Credit and Project Summary
Borrower:             The Republic of Kenya.
Implementiny A2ency
and Beneficia:       National Water Conservation and Pipeline Corporation (NWCPC).
Credit Amount:        SDR31.8 million (US$43.2 million equivalent).
C     zit Terms:      Standard IDA Terms with a maturity of 40 years.
OnIgnding T.rnms:     US$17.4 million equivalent for rehabilitation/augmentation works and for
vehicles and equipment as a loan to NWCPC at an interest rate fixed at the
Treasury Bill yield on the date of the auction closest to the date of
commitment of the IDA Credit (currently 16.5 percent per annum), to be
repaid over 20 years including 5 years grace. The balance of the Credit
(US$25.8 million equivalent) for institutional support and preinvestment
studies would be transferred to NWCPC as an equity contribution by the
Government.
Project Description:  The Project would help finance the preparation of a follow-on Mombasa and
Coastal Water Supply Project. The main components of the Project are:
(a) institutional support to the National Water Conservation and Pipeline
Corporation to ensure its institutional, operational and financial viability for
carrying out the proposed Project in particular and for fulfilling its mandate
in the water supply sector in general; (b) pre-investment studies and detailed
engineering designs for the extension of the Mombasa and Coastal Water
Supply System and the related waste water disposal systems for improved
provision of safe water and for improved sanitation for the rapidly
expanding population; and (c) rehabilitation/augmentation of existing
facilities to reduce the water supply deficit (presently about 60 percent) in
the near term. The Project would also ensure that adverse environmental
impacts of the follow-on Project (Class EA) and the augmentation works
under this Project (Class EB) would be minimized.
frtQIt.t fitks:       The introduction of an appropriate tariff policy which is expected to require
significant increases in current water tariffs could be difficult to achieve in
v.ew of political constraints. To reduce this risk, the Project would provide
for carrying out a tariff study and for strengthening NWCPC's revenue
collection and financial management systems. In addition, there would be
annual financial reviews and tariff increases necessary to meet the following
year's requirements would be agreed and implemented. Government's firm
commitment to the Project and to the objective of establishing an
independent, self-financing corporation was demonstrated by an initial tariff
increase of about 50 percent, versus the minimum 20 percent requested by
IDA. The introduction of a differentiated, block tariff system lessened the
impact of tariff increases on low income groups and would help to defuse



(iv)
the political/social issues of increasing the cost of water for the consumers.
Inadequate allocation in the Government's development budget for the IDA
Credit and counterpart funds due to financial and budgetary constraints
would be another risk. To reduce this risk a detailed schedule of the
counterpart fund requirements had been prepared jointly with NWCPC and
Treasury, and agreement was reached that Government's contribution would
be deposited on the project account in a time', manner. The initial deposit
required for FY91/92 was made a condition of Credit effectiveness. Some
unforeseen difficulties could arise fronm a relatively new institution in spite
of its qualified staff, but these difficulties are expected to be reduced
through institutional support and the hiring of consultants to assist in project
preparation and implementation activities. Monitoring of NWCPC's
performance in these areas would be a focus of IDA supervision.

KENYA
SECOND MOMBASA AND COASTAL WATER SUPPLY
ENGINEERING AND REHABILITATION PROJECT
1. THE WATER SUPPLY SECTOR
Country Background
1.01         The Republic of Kenya, stretching inland from the Indian Ocean to Lake
Victoria, covers 583,000 Km' and straddles the equator. Along the Indian Ocean coastline
lies a narrow tropical belt, behind which an arid plain extend to the north. Toward the
northwest, the land rises to a high plateau, the Kenya Highlands, at about 1,500 m above sea
level with a temperate climate and subtropical vegetation. The Highlands are bisected from
north to south by the Rift Valley.
1.02         Kenya's population is now about 21 million, with one of the highest
population growth rates (about 3.6 percent per annum) in the world. The rural popula2ion IS
estimated at 18 million, while 3 million people live in the rapidly growing urban areas, The
capital city, Nairobi has a population of about 1.3 million. About 85 percent of the
population and the majority of economic enterprises are concentrated on the Highlands
plateau. Mombasa is the second largest city and the principal seaport in Kenya with a
population of about 0.8 million. Mombasa and the Coastal Regior. is a significant and rapidly
growing base of the tourism industry. The total population of the Coastal Region is estimated
at 1.6 million.
1.03         Kenya's per capita income of US$368 in 1990 places it at the higher end of
the range for low-income countries. The economy is heavily dependent on agriculture, which
employs about eighty percent of the labor force, contributos more than one quarter of GDP,
and accounts for close to one half of merchandise exports. The manufacturing sector
contributes about 12 percent of GDP, and manufactured exports are low at less than 20
percent of total exports. The services sector accounts for about one half of GDP and is an
important source of employment. It includes tourism which in recent years has become
Kenya's leading foreign exchange earner. The tourism industry accounts for more than 50
percent of Kenya's foreign earnings, most of which is generated in the Coast Region.
1.04         Kenya has enjoyed an economic recovery over the past five years, reversing
the relative stagnation of the first half of the 1980s. Government's aims for FY92 through
FY94 are to attain output growth of over 5 percent per annum, to reduce the inflation to the
average level forecast for Kenya's main trading partners (about 5 percent per annum) and to
reduce the external current account deficit to a sustainable level of 4.4 percent of GDP. To
achieve these targets the budget deficit needs to be substantially reduced through the
rationalization of public expenditures and parastatal reform, the trade regime must be
liberalized and exports promoted by foreign exchange and incentive policies and the use of
non-concessional sources of external financing must be limited. During the November 1991
Consultative Group meeting, donors expressed their concerns about the extent of
Government's commitment to major structural reforms and linked continued high levels of
quick disbursing assistance to concrete actions. This project is designed to support parastatal
reform, and to reduce Government subventions in the water supply sector; and to protect the
earning capacity of the coastal region, especially in tourism, through improved water supply
infrastructure.



-2-
Water Resources
1.05         Kenya experiences wide differences in climate caused mainly by variations in
altitude. It has two distinct rainy seasons and two dry seasons. The hottest months are
December through March which are followed by the "long rains" until early June. The
Kenya "winter" is from late June until early September and this is followed by the "short
rains" which often last until early December. The coastal strip is dominated by the twice
yearly monsoons which blow to and from the Arabian Gulf. Average annual rainfall Is about
450 mm, but the actual distribution means that vast areas receive 250 mm or less per annum.
As a result of the rainfall pattern, streamflows a.e highly seasonal and vary widely. Perennial
rivers are concentrated in the Highlands area (including Nairobi) and the coastal zones. The
groundwater potential, except in the eastern part of the country, appears to be limited, and
considerably more investigation is required to identify and develop this source for large scale
water supplies. Unfortunately, in some areas, groundwater is saline and in other, including
Nairobi and the Rift Valley, groundwater contains a high level of fluorides. In tlhe southern
part of Kenya, near the Tanzanian border, there are some large natural springs where the
water in the permeable volcanic rocks (lava-flows and volcanic tuffs) comes to the surface.
Some of these springs are utilized for large water supply schemes.
Access to Water in Urban Areas
1.06         While most of the urban population in Kenya has access to piped water, the
urban population growth rate of 7 percent strains the existing capacity of the systems. At
present, water is supplied through metered individual connection to about 70 percent of the
urban population and to about 8 percent through communal water points or water selling
kiosks. The remaining 22 percent of the population has only limited indirect access to potable
water. Overall, urban water usage (excluding Nairobi) ranges from 70 l.p.c.d. to about 160
I.p.c.d. (including industrial consumption). Nairobi's average is about 180 l.p.c.d., which is
normal for this environment. The per capita consumption in Mombasa should be about the
same (160 to 180 l.p.c.d.), as a result of the high number of tourists.
Acces 1qWater in Rural Area
1.07         Only 2.6 million people (about 15 percent of the rural population) are supplied
with safe drinking water. The rest of the rural population has to carry water over
considerable distances from natural sources which are normally polluted, inadequate or dry
for several weeks each year. Water usage ranges from 50 I.p.c.d for individual connections
(about 47,000 to serve 600,000 people) down to about 6 I.p.c.d. where water is being carried.
Although a large number of water supply schemes have been constructed under the country's
Rural Water Supply Program, many with external donor assistance, the level of operation and
maintenance has been inadequate and many schemes are in need of rehabilitation.
Sewerage and Sanitation
1.08         Therf are 22 public sewerage systems in Kenya, servicing about 6 percent of
the total population. Another 4 percent have septic tanks and 40 percent make use of pit
latrines. The remaining 50 percent have no proper sanitation facilities. In Nairobi, about 50
percent of the population is served by public sewer systems, and 20 percent by septic tanks.



--3--
The Ministry of Local Government (MLG) and the Ministry of Water Development (MWD)
have undertaken improvements in other municipalities and smaller towns. The sewerage and
sanitation situation in Mombasa (built-up urban areas) is described in paras 2.14 through 2.17.
Operation and maintenance by local authorities, except in Nairobi, are often to very low
standards.
Sectgr Legislation
1.09         The Water Act, revised in 1986, and currently under review, together with Its
subsidiary legislation, covers the broad field of conservation, control, appointment and use of
water resources. The Water Apportionment Board (WAB) is responsible for issuing all water
abstraction licenses in Kenya. It is able to vary and revoke awards of water use permits.
MWD provides the Board with technical advice, manpower for the Board's Secretariat, and
water bailiffs who are the Board's field workers. The chairman of WAB is appointed by the
Minister of Water Development.
1.10         Complementary to the Water Act is the Public H{ealth Act which includes
many provisions relating to water and sewerage from the health point of view. The Local
Government Act, Cap. 265, gives power to local authorities in urban areas to undertake water
supply, sewerage and drainage works, to maintain waterworks and to make appropriate by-
laws.
Sector Qrganization
1.11         Reflecting the growing importance which the Government attaches to the
water sector and to rural water supply in particular, over the past two decades responsibility
for water supply has passed from the Ministry of Works to the Ministry of Natural Resources
to the Ministry of Agriculture, and finally to the MWD, which was established in 1974 to
concentrate on the development of the water sector. In addition, under the Ministry of
Energy (MOE) and the Ministry of Regional Development (MORD), five river basin
development authorities (the Tana and Athi Rivers, Lake Basin, Kerio Valley, Uaso Nyiro
North and Uaso Nyiro South) were established as independent statutory bodies. They are
responsible for the planning and implementation of integrated, mtulti-sectoral development
projects and orderly water exploitation in their respective areas. Major irrigation
developments are the responsibility of the National Irrigation Board.
1.12         Since its establishment, the MWD has concentrated on the rural water supply
sector by constructing and operating several hundred schemes under the first four phases of its
Rural Development Program, with varying degrees of success. In addition to these MWD
schemes, other ministries and bodies have made rural water investments. The county councils
operate with limited success several hundred small schemes which are scheduled to be
gradually taken over by MWD as funding permits. Kenya Railways also operates about a
hundred schemes which supply water to adjacent villages in addition to their own
requirements. The Ministry of Land and Settlement plans and finances water supply for new
settlement areas, usually with MWD's Water Department as the executing agency. "Self-
help' water supply schemes in rural areas are overseen by MWD.



1.13         The National Water Conservation and Pipeline Corporation (NWCPC) was
established under the State Corporation Act (Cap 446) of Parliament in 1988 as an
autonomous agency reporting to MWD and became operational on July 1, 1989. Its prime
function is to manage and operate major water supply schemes (previously operated by
MWD) on a conmmercial basis, and to develop and implernent new water supply projects. (see
also paras 4.01 through 4.03). As of June, 1991 45 schemes have been transferred from
MWD to NWCPC (Map IBRD 23240 and Annex 6). This includes the Mombasa and Coastal
Water Supply schemes (but the Mombasa sewerage services are the Mombasa municipality's
responsibility).
1.14         In the rest of the urban sector, the Nairobi water supply and sewerage system
Is owned by the Nairobi City Commission (NCC) and operated by its Water and Sewerage
Department (WSD), and five other major municipalities operate their own water supply
systems under the direction of MLG with technical guidance from MWD) and NWCPC.
Sector Development Program
1.15         Kenya's water sector objective, as stated in the 1974 National Water Master
Plan, is to ensure that every household has potable water within a distance of four kilometers
by the year 2000. In the early 1970's, government launched a Rural Water Supply Program
to speed rural development, which is now in its fourth phase. The Government also endorsed
the targets of the International Drinking Water Supply Decade and, in October 1980, created
a National Action Committee to coordinate Decade planning. Annual development
expenditure by MWD in the water supply and sanitation sector grew very rapidly in the
1970s, to about US$25 million equivalent by 1979. Since then, actual expenditure has
dropped below this figure due to financial constraints, but emphasis is still given to the rural
areas, especially to those projects which have attracted substantiai amounts of concessional
donor finance. Sewerage and sanitation developmnent expenditures amount to only 10 percent
of sector expenditures.
1.16         The rural water sector has benefitted substantially from both grants and
technical assistance provided by various international donor agencies. Over 60 percent of
MWD's Rural Water Supply Program has been financed by the nordic donor agencies (SIDA,
NORAD, DANIDA and FINNIDA), the Netherlands, CIDA and KfW. In the past, this
funding was concentrated on new projects to benefit the unserved population. While this
program was largely successful, because of the rapid expansion MWD was unable to provide
necessary back-up services and finance to operate and maintain these new facilities.
Recognizing these constraints, the donors are now focussing their assistance on rehabilitation,
strengdtening of operations and maintenance, and technical assistance to MWD. The recent
trend in rural water supply strategy focuses on village based operations, community
participation and appropriate technology.
1.17         The most significant change in the sector development strategy is the
recognition by Government of the principle that water users have to pay the full cost for the
services provided to them. This has very important implications for the restructuring of the
water supply sector, The first step in this direction was the creation of NWCPC which,
according to its mandate, has to operate the water supply schemes transferred under its
jurisdiction on a commercial basis, without goverrnent subsidy. There is also a commitnent



-- - -
to rehabilitate and upgrade the existing schemes so that they can operate at optimal level.
NWCPC will also assist the Government in the formulation of a national water development
policy which will inter alia clarify the tariff policy to be applied to rural water users. In rural
water schemes water charges traditionally aim at covering operation and maintenance costs.
A study on the National Water Master Plan (financed by JICA) is presently being prepared.
Develoment Constraints and Problem Areas
1.18         The major constraints to an effective restructuring of the water supply sector
are twofold: financial and Institutional. The new policy that NWCPC has to manage
establishment,yoperation and maintenance of water supply schemes without recourse to
Government subsidies requires that the Corporation revenues collected from water users cover
all costs. The present revenue level falls far short of this requirement, hence a number of
actions are needed to raise revenue. First, the level and structure of the water tariffs charged
to users have to be set in such a way that they cover both recurrent and capital costs, while 2t
the same time giving due consideration to social objectives including availability of nminimum
quantities of ;vater at affordable prices to lower income groups. Second, the billing and
collection systems have to be strengthened so that a high proportion of water supplied is
effectively billed and bills collected. Third, an important condition for user cooperation with
the revenue drive is that the Corporation will deliver reliable and good auality water supply.
Moreover, more efficient operation of services can help bring down the cost of water supply
and increase the net revenues. Government has recognized the need to improve the
Corporations's cost recovery as well as operational efficiency in order to overcome the
financial constraint, and also the need for institutional strengthening of this new agency so that
the Corporation can perform operational as well as financial activities more effectively. The
Corporation has identified systematic manpower development and specific training programs
as major tools to overcome the institutional constraints.
Previous Bank rrowp Projects and Lessons Learned
1.19         The Bank has been involved in the urban and water sector since 1970.
During this period, the Bank has financed three urban projects and six water supply projects.
The Nairobi Sites and Services Project (Loan 1205-KE)/Credit 543-KB) included funds for a
substantial sanitation and sewerage comportent to benefit the urban poor in Nairobi. The
Second Urban Project (Loan 1550-KE/Credit 791-KE) undertook to finance site and service
developments and urban upgrading in Nairobi, Mombasa and Kisumu. The Secondary Towns
Project (Loan 2319-KE/Credit 1390-KE) assisted the development in Kitale, Eldoret, Nyeri,
Thika and Nakuru, with the aim of developing an integrated urban network to support
economic growth in the rural areas and improving the urban employment base of secondary
towns.
1.20         The Nairobi Water Supply Project (Loan 714-KE) implemented during 1972-
76 with Bank assistance was the first phase of a long-range program to develop the Chania-
Kimakia-Thika river system for the purpose of meeting Nairobi's water bupply requirements.
This was followed by the Second Nairobi Water Supply Project (Loan 1520-KE),
implemented during 1979-84, to provide the necessary extensions for Nairobi's water supply
up to 1987-88. The Third Nairobi Water Supply Engineering Project (Credit 1566-KE)
assisted the Government nd the Nairobi City Commission (NCC) and its Water Sewerage



--6--
DepartmenL (WSD) in the preparation of the next extension phase and strengthening of the
institutional and financial capacity of the implementing agency (NCC/WSD). The Tird
Nairobi Water Supply Project (Credit 2060-KE) is presently being implemented. The Bank
also financed (together with KfM) the Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Project (Loan
1167-KE), which was implemernted in 1972-79 by MWD. The Nairobi water supply projects
have been successfully implemented albeit with cost overruns of varying degrees. The
Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Project was less successful: due to inadequate
preparatory work and high cost overruns, some of the project components had to be canceled,
and the capacity of the new scheme is about 10 percent below the design capacity.
1.21         The Bank has made one loan to the rural water sector, namely the Rural
Water Supply Project (Loan 1637-KE) in 1977. Due to the problems of implementation and
:eration which MWD was experiencing with the Rural Water Supply Program, the Bank-
financed project got off to a very slow start. In 1983, the project was restructured and
reduced at Government's request. Since then, Government's continuing financial constraints
have adversely affected MWD's financial and physical capacity to absorb any further
responsibilities, and the project was finally canceled on mutdal agreement in February 1985.
1.22         In assessing the lessons learned from previous Bank group involvement in the
water sector, especially in large urban water supply proi--,ts, it is clear that detailed project
preparation is a necessary condition for the success of such investments. The first Mombasa
and Coastal Water Supply Project was appraised too early in the project cycle (without final
engineering designs) which contributed to original underestimation of quantities and unit costs
and an unrealistic implementation schedule which distorted the appraisal estimates. This
resulted in prolonged implementation and high cost overrun, and some of the originally
planned project components had to be deleted from the program. In contrast, the Nairobi
Project was appraised after thorough project preparation and detailed designs (carried out
under an engineering project) which ensured a realistic project formulation and a favorable
cofinancing arrangement. The same phased approach is being followed in the case of the
proposed project (s< para 3.04).
1.23         The other key lessons learned from the past projects -- which will be
addressed in this operation -- are that the implementing agency should be financially viable
and autonomous; institutional improvement and capacity building should be emphasized in
project formulation; and the implementation of Government's stated policy regarding cost
recovery on water supply projects should be consistently pursued and followed up beyond the
physical completion of individual projects.
1.24         The Bank group strategy is to support Government's efforts to achieve its
targets in water sector development by providing financial assistance for high priority, water
supply projects with continuing assistance for institution building, emphasizing manpower
development and improvement in administrative and financial management. The Bank
therefore contitihes to assist the expansion of water supply and sewerage systems in Nairobi,
the nation's capital and in Mombasa (and the coastal region) which is the second largest
industrial town in Kenya and also an important center of tourism, to which the government
attaches high priGrity.



-7-
IL. THE PROJECT AREA - MOMBUSA AND THE COASTAL REGION
LQoation and Features
2.01         The Coast Province covers about 83,000 km2, or one seventh &f the total area of
Kenya. Its population is estimated at 1.6 million. Many of the Coast Province inhabitants are
concentrated in urban areas, particularly Mombasa, Kilifi and Malindi along the coast. Because
of a warm climate and an abundance of excellent beaches, tourism along the coastal strip from the
Tanzanian border to northeast to Malindi has grown rapidly in recent years.
2.02         Mombasa, the second largest city in Kenya, located on the South-eastern coast is
developing steadily. It is the principal seaport of Kenya, which also serves other land-locked
countries of Eastern Africa. Mombasa is also a center of transport2tion, trade and indust.y.
Duzring the recent decades Mombasa has grown to become the coulitry's major tourist gate-town,
functioning as the center of the coastal resort belt which attracts many foreign visitors. The
municipal area comprises Mombasa Island, the North Mainland, the South Mainland and the West
Mainland. The business center is on the Island which is linked to the North Mainland by the
Nyali EBidge and to the West Mainland by a causeway. The connection between the Island and
the South Mainland is provided by ferry service. rhe North and South Mainland areas are
primarily residential, with a large number of hotels along the beaches. Industrial and commercial
activities center on the port and its allied facilities, oil refineries, brewery and cement plant and a
variety of light industries, located mostly on the Island and West Mainland.
Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply System
2.03         The water supply in NWCPC's Coast Region is unique in Kenya in the sense that,
except for the Taveta-Lumi scheme and some minor, community based operations, the whole
region is supplied through an integrated, regional water supply system. This service area includes
Mombasa municipality and its environs, the coastal strip north of Mombasa up to Malindi, the
south coast area, Kwale town, and an about 10 kIn wide strip of occupied land along the existing
Mzima-pipeline and the Mombasa-Nairobi roai and railway line between Voi and Mazeras.
2.04         For operational purposes, the service area is organized into 8 sub-areas (IBRD
Map 23241):

All sources are ope ating near their maximum output with the exception of the Baricho Intake
Works (and treatment plant) which has a theoretical output of 72,000 m3lday. This amount,
however, cannot be obtained at present due to problems of siltation within the intake and due to
limitations in pump capacity (see also para. 2.07 (d)).
2.06         Groundwater investigations have been carried out for about four years near
Baricho, to supplement the production of the existing intake (financed by KfW). These
investigations located the paleo-river channel of the Sabaki River, where indications of very
significant quantities of good quality groundwater were obtained. Two 60 m deep large diameter
wells are presently under construction, with an expected yield of 10,000 m3/day each. The
proposed Project would provide for the continuation/extension of the hydrogeological
investigations. Based on the available data, it is expected that with the construction of a new
wellfield on the left bank (flood plain) of the Sabaki river, an additional 40,000 m3/day can be
made available from this source.
2.07         All bulk water supply schemes (source works, treatment plants and transmission
mains) are in need of rehabilitation. The major problems are:
(a)    Mzima Springs: The source works (spring intake structure), built in 1952, is
operational at present supply capacity, but additional works would be required if
this source is to be expanded. The 220 km long gravity transmission main and its
auxiliary structures (break-pressure tanks) need rehabilitation, especially near Voi,
where frequent pipe bursts are experienced due to nfavorable soil conditions.
(b)    Marere Springs: Some rehabilitation of the source works is required. The 40 km
long transmission main (laid in 1916) needs replacement.
(c)    The TIWIboeh_o1es are well maintained but investigation should be carried out
regarding the danger of saline water intrusion into the aquifer.
(d)      3Barijbo Intake (and t1eatment plant) on the Sabaki River, built in the late
1970's (under the first Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Project), did not fulfill
the expectations. IExcept a short initial period, the source works didn't operate at
design capacity, due to siltation (and silt removal) problems within the intake and



- 9 -
frequent breakdown of the raw water pumps. The existing water treatment plant
is in serious state of disrepair and needs rehabilitation. The transmission main
was originally designed to carry about 72,000 m3/day water, with two booster
pumping stations of which only one was constructed at Jaribuni. This pumping
station needs rehabilitation, If the production from this source can be increased,
an additional booster pumping station is required near Didima.
(e)    The Kwa-Alenva Intaks (and treatment plant) on the Sabaki River was the first
source for Malindi. It operates around its design capacity, but the works are old
and badly in need of rehabilitation if they are to be retained.
Water Distribution
2.08        'The first piped water supply to Mombasa was provided from the Marere Springs
(built in 1916), through the Changamwe terminal reservoir. The distribution network was
gradually expanded and in the mid 1950s a second source (bulk supply system) was added to the
system based on the Mzima Springs. The transmission main from Mzima delivers water to the
Mazeras terminal reservoir which has been interconnected to the Changamwe reservoir. The
present network was designed to distribute the water available from these two sources, i.e. about
47,000 m3/day. Although some additional network strengthening was implemented in the
following years, no major, systematic distribution network extension was carried out in Mombasa
since this time.
2.09         The development of the Baricho source works on the Sabaki River and the
transmission main which became operational in 1979, brought about an additional 35,000 m3/day
water to the Mombasa syst,m. However, due to the high cost overrun of this project and the lack
of funds in the following years prevented the upgrading and extension of the distribution system,
originally planned as part of the project.
2.10         The present water shortage in Mombasa is furiter aggravated by the aged and
inadequate pipe network, which cannot evenly distribute even the presently available water. Pipe
bursts are frequent in some zones with higher pressure and other areas receive water only for a
few hours per day. Due to lack of any reliable data-base, unaccounted for water in the system
cannot be calculated, but it is estimated to be 30 to 40 percent at least. A full review, redesign,
and rehabilitation and expansion of the distribution system is urgently needed.
2.11         Presently there are about 71,000 consumer connections in the system. The annual
average increase in connections during the last few years has been about 2000 and mostly in the
North- and South-Mainland for new developments (hotels and residential areas). In principle, all
connections are metered but most of the meters are non functiornal. In addition, consumer
registration records are also unreliable. The metered connections include about 160 water selling
kiosks.
2.12         The distribution networks in the other urban areas served by the system (Kilifi,
Malindi, Kwale and Likoni) are generally in better condition but extension of the reticulation
systems in these locations are also needed to keep pace with the rapid growth.



10 -
Water Demand
2.13         The latest water demand projections were prepared in 1975 as part of the
preparation of the first Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Project. Based on this forecast, the
present water demand for Mombasa and the coastal region serviced by the existing regional
scheme would be about 146,000 m3/day. It is presently estimated that the average effective
demand, due to the faster growth of the coastal region than anticipated, is about 160,000 to
170,000 m3/day (equivalent to an average of about 100 l.p.c.d for the population of about 1.64
million) versus the present average bulk supply of 85,400 m3/day, showing a water deficit of
about 50 percent. Including physical losses in the system, specifically is Mombasa (estimated at
30 percent), the water deficit is over 60 percent. Detailed analysis of the present water demand
and preparation of a demand forecast is needed, and will be part of the consultancy services
included in the proposed Project.
Existing Sewerage and Drainage Systems
2.14         The responsibility for sewerage, drainage and sanitation in Mombasa and the other
urban centers along the coast rests with the municipalities, as part of their urban services. In the
Coast Province, sewerage and drainage systems exist only in Mombasa serving a part of
Mombasa Island and West Mainland. The systems cover approximately 15 percent of the
estimated district population. Other disposal systems for human waste are on-site sanitation
systems comprising of pit latrines and septic tanks, which cover about 60 percent and 25 percent
of the population, respectively. None of the sewage treatment works are currently in operation,
and raw sewage is disposed directly into the sea through the sewers or watercourses. Pit latrines
are used in the high density, Swahili-type housing areas, and septic tanks are used in the areas
where piped water is available, including all the tourist hotels along the coast, and most of the
commercial and industrial areas. However, these on-site sanitation systems often pollute the
ground water and are often flooded by storm water due to insufficient drainage coverage and
improper maintenance of the drainage systems, thereby posing serious health risks.
2.15         The Mombasa Island sewerage system was commissioned in 1963 and covers the
Old Town and the east part of the town center. The system consists of 20 km of sewer pipes, 2.3
km of tunnels, four pumping stations, a primary treatment plant in the underground of the
Mombasa Golf Course and a marine outfall at Ras Serani. The primary treatment system includes
a screening and a sedimentation process which are now out of operation due to mechanical
problems. Therefore, raw sewage is being discharged through an outfall tunnel directly into the
mouth of Mombasa harbor without any treatment. Even when the plant was operating in the past,
sedimented sludge generated at the plant was directly discharged into the sea without any
treatment. Furthermore, of the four pumping stations on the Island, three pumping stations are
out of operation due to pipe blockages and mechanical problems, and again, raw sewage is being
directly discharged through by-pass channels into the middle of Mombasa harbor. Therefore, the
Island sewerage system is functioning simply for draining sewage but providing no treatment
before discharging it to the sea. The Mombasa-Island drainage system covers the most densely
developed areas, but grey water spillage on the street and street flooding often result from
blockages in the drainage system.
2.16         The West Mainland sewerage system was commissioned in 1972 to serve the
Changamwe housing estate and industrial estates. The system includes 9.4 km of sewers, an
oxydation ditch treatment facility and sludge drying beds. Currently, the treatment plant's four
rotors for mixing and oxygen supply are rusted and impaired, thereby rendering the plant



- 11 -
Inoperative, with dhe result that raw sewage is by-passed through the drainage channel to the
Kilindini harbor. However, with assistance from the Saudi Fund, the Municipal Council of
Mombasa is currently implementing a project to rehabilitate and upgrade the treatment plant and
extend the sewer main, which is expected to be commissioned by the end of 1992. The new plant
will consist of inlet works, 2 extended aeration tanks, 4 final settling tanks, 2 sludge thickening
tanks and 23 sludge drying beds, and is expected to treat 17,400 m3/day of raw sewage to an
acceptable level of treatment for discharging into the harbor. Four pumping stations are also
under construction in the extension project, located at Mikindani, Port Reitz, Miritini site and
service area, and Miritani pumping station. Due to budgetary constraints, the project does not
include any lateral sewers at this time. The West Mainland drainage system covers the
Changamwe area, but the condition is the same as in Mombasa Island where grey water spillage
on the street and street flooding are common.
2.17          The current sewerage tariff is co;iected together with the water tariff by NWCPC
on behalf of the Municipality. The official FY91 sewerage tariff is based on water consumption,
ranging from 2.5 to 4.0 KSh per m3 of water consumption. However, NWCPC's computer
billing program has not been properly updated and the very low flat rates of 15 Ksh/month for
domestic users; 20 Ksh/month for commercial users and 30 Ksh/month for trade users are
actually being levied. The official sewerage tariff is about 40 percent of the current water tariff,
but traditionally sewer tariffs are higher than water tariffs because sewerage systems are generally
more expensive than water supply systems. In Mombasa the actual tariff collected for the
sewerage services is even lower than the official tariff due to the flat rate charge. Thus, the
revenue for the Municipality for sewefage services is estimated to cover only a small portion of
operation and maintenance costs.
2.18          The Project will provide for consultancy services: (a) to assess in detail the waste
water disposal, drainage and sanitation situation in Mombasa and the other urban centers along
the coast with special regard to the impact of the increased supply of water (under the proposed
Project and the follow-on main investment project) and to assist the municipalities in preparing
short- and long-term plans for upgrading their related services; (b) to prepare a phased
development plan for sewerage and drainage in Mombasa with special regard to Mombasa Island
and West-Mainland; (c) to prepare detailed engineering designs for the rehabilitation, upgrading
and extension of these services commensurate with the proposed extension of water supply
services.



12 -
III, THE PROlECT
Genesmi
3.01         Mombasa has had piped water supply since 1916 and the capacity of the
system was adequate to satisfy the demands until the late 1970s. The first Mombasa and
Coastal Water Supply Project, implemented in 1972-79, increased the production and
transmission capacities of the piped water supply system in the project area from about 46,000
m3/day to the present 85,400 m3/day, but has fallen short of the originally planned total
capacity of 100,000 m3/day which was expected to meet the demands up to about 1983.
Indeed, water shortages began first in 1982-83 and water rationing became necessary since
1984-85. At present, Mombasa and the coastal region are suffering from a very serious water
shortage of about 50 percent of requirements, while the water demand is increasing by about
5 percent per annum.
3.02         The Ministry of Water Development (MWD) carried out a "Feasibility Study
on Water Supply Augmentation Project of Mombasa - Coastal Area and the Hinterland" as
early as 1981 (financed by JICA), as a first step to prepare for a next water supply extension
project for the region. Based on this study, the National Water Resources and Pipeline
Corporation (to whom the responsibility for the water supply in the Mombasa and Coast
Region had been transferred in 1988) concluded that the next viable option was to build a
second transmission main from Mzima Springs and increase the abstraction from this source.
NWCPC, in March 1989, issued an "Invitation for firm proposals for provision of
engineering and design services, construction of works and financing of the Second Mzima
Pipeline Project" to nine selected internationally qualified civil engineering contractors. This
turn-key approach did not succeed mainly because the required concessional funding for the
project could not be secured.
3.03         In late January 1990, the Vice President and Minister for Finance of Kenya
requested the Bank to consider coordinating the funding for this project and to participate
itself in the project with IDA funding. Following an exchange of letters between the Bank
and the Government, a Bank reconnaissance mission visited Kenya in May 1990, to get
acquainted with the situation in Mombasa and the Coast Region and with the proposed
project, and to establish direct contact with NWCPC, the implementing agency.
3.04         The mission proposed a two-step approach: first, an engineering and
rehabilitation project, under which assistance would be provided for (i) institutional
improvement/capacity building for NWCPC; (ii) preparation of pre-investment studies and
engineering; and (iii) rehabilitation/augmentation works to improve the water supply in the
near term. Second, the engineering credit would be followed by a main investment project.
3.05         In a ,etter, signed by the Vice President and Minister for Finance, dated
October 18, 1990, the Government formally requested IDA assistance for an Engineering
Credit on the basis of the proposal of the May 1990 reconnaissance mission. The World
Bank acknowledged the request on October 25, 1990 and followed up the matter with the
Ministry of Water Development (MWD), responsible for water resources development in
Kenya. As a result of the follow-up discussions MWD proposed that a Government
delegation would visit Washington to discuss the modalities and steps to be taken in the
preparation of the proposed Project.



- 13 -
3.06         The Kenyan delegation, led by the Permanent Secretary, MWD and with the
participation of officials from Treasury and NWCPC visited Washington February 25 through
March 1, 1990. During the discussions agreement was reached, in principle, regarding the
objectives, scope and major components of the proposed Project, and a Project processing
timetable.
3.07         Jn accordance with the agreed timetable, a preparation/preappraisal mission
visited Kenya in April 1990, and the proposed Project was formally appraised in the field
from June 17 through July 13, 1991.
3.08          The proposed Project would be the first step towards the improvement and
extension of the water supply and waste water disposal systems in Mombasa and the Coastal
Region, to be followed by a much larger investment project. The main objectives of the
Project are:
(a)    to provide support to NWCPC to help ensure its institutional,
operational and financial viability for carrying out the proposed
preparatory project and the follow-on main project in particular, and
for fulfilling its broad mandate in the water suply sector in general;
(b)    to carry out preinvestment studies and engineering designs for the
extensioh of the Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply System and the
related waste water disposal systems, that would (i) improve provision
of safe water and better sanitation to the rapidly expanding population,
(ii) protect the earning capacity of the coastal region, especially in
tourism, and (iii) allow for economic growth;
(c)    to reduce the water supply deficit (presently about 60 percent) in the
near term, through rehabilitation/augmentation of existing facilities;
(d)    to ensure that adverse environmental impacts of the follow-on project
and of the rehabilitation/augmentation works under this Project would
be minimnized.

Cost Estimates
3.10          The total Project cost is estimated at KSh 1,461.0 million (US$48.7 million
equivalent) of which KSh 986.6 million (US$32.9 million equivalent) or about 68 percent is the
foreign exchange component. Local taxes are estimated at about KSh 21.0 million and are
included in the cost estimates. A letter from the Office of the Vice President and Ministry of
Finance (Ref. No.: CONF. 3/03, dated June 15, 1991) confirmed NWCPC's exemption from the
payment of import taxes and duties, which was a condition for negotiations; therefore, import
taxes and duties are not included in the cost estimates. Base costs are expressed in-January 1991
prices. Summary project cost estimates are shown in Table 3.1 and detailed cost estimates are
given in Annex 2, Tables 1 to 4.
3.11          Project costs were estimated by NWCPC and the Bank appraisal team, based on
similar works recently implemented in Kenya, and recent quotations for purchase of vehicles and
equipment. The land acquisition cost is the estimated purchase price of land to be acquired for
the augmentation works (well field near Baricho), including compensation costs for crops. The
engineering and supervision costs are estimated at 8 percent and 5 percent of the cost of the
rehabilitation/augmentation works, respectively. Physical contingencies are estimated at 10
percent for the rehabilitation and augmentation (physical) works and at 5 percent for consultancy
and advisory services. Price contingencies for local and foreign currency costs are based on
expected price increases as shown below:

In total, physical contingencies are estimated at about 7 percent of base cost, and price
contingencies are estimated at about 14 percent of base cost plus physical contingencies.

3.12         The Project cost estimate is based on a five-year implementation period. This is

Project Financing A-rrangements
3.13         The proposed IDA credit of US$43.2 million (KSh 1,296 million) would finance
about 89 percent of total project cost or 90 percent of total project cost net of taxes. Agreement
was reached during negotiations that the remaining 11 percent or about US$5.5 million equivalent
(KSh 165 million) of total project cost would be provided by Government as an equity
contribution to NWCPC, which as a new institution, has limited capability to contribute to the
capital cost of the project.
3.14          The IDA Credit would be made to the Republic of Kenya (ROK). The portion of
the IDA Credit utilized to finance consultancy/advisory services under the institutional support
component, training, and preinvestment studies/engineering services for the follow-on project
(UJS$ 25.8 million equivalent) would be provided to NWCPC as an equity contribution by the
Government and the balance utilized to finance rehabilitation/augmentation works (including
associated engineering services), vehicles and equipment (US$ 17.4 million equivalent) would be
passed on to NWCPC as a loan. In line with the Kenyan Government's prior agreement with
IDA for on-lending to parastatals under the Financial Sector Adjustment Credit, it was agreed
during negotiations that the loan to NWCPC would carry an interest rate, fixed at the Treasury
Bill yield on the date of the auction closest to the date of negotiations of the IDA Credit (16.65%
per annum, as of October 18, 1991). The loan to NWCPC would have a maturity of 20 years
which would allow for a 5-year grace period corresponding to the implementation period of the
project and a repayment period of 15 years. As for past water projects, the foreign exchange risk
of the IDA Credit be borne by ROK. The execution of the Subsidiary Loan Agreement between
the Government and NWCPC would be a condition of Credit effectiveness.
3.15          For the unhindered and timely execution of the Project, it is important that both
the budgetary allocation for the IDA Credit and counterpart funds are ensured for each fiscal year
during the implementation period in the Government's development budget, as summarized in
Table 3.2 below and shown in detail in Annex 2, Table 4. During negotiations, agreement was
reached that Government would

3.16         The National Water Conservation and Pipeline Corporation (NWCPC) would be
responsible for implementation of the proposed Project. It was agreed during appraisal that a
Project Implementation Unit (PIU) will be set up in NWCPC to carry out the tasks related to the
implementation of major investment projects (contractual and procurement matters, supervision of
consultancy services and physical works, disbursement matters, project accounting and project
reporting), as a permanent part of the organization. 'Te PIU will be headed by the Project
Coordinator who will report to the Managing Director and will liaise with NWCPC's other
departments. The Project Coordinator will be assisted by an advisor, employed under the
Coordination Liaison/Management Consultancy Services and financed under the Project. These
services would also provide assistance in establishing an on-the-job training program for the PIU.
The PIU at NWCPC's Headquarters will be manned initially by an engineer and an accountant
(with support staff) and will have a branch office in NWCPC's Regional office in Mombasa,
under the direct supervision of the Regional Manager. This unit in Mombasa would consist of a
technician and an accounting staff. NWCPC would retain consultants for the institutional studies
and for the preinvestment studies and engineering as well as for the engineering and construction
supervision of the rehabilitation and augmentation works. During negotiations, agreement was
reached that, prior to Credit effectiveness, NWCPC would establish and thereafter would maintain
the Project Implementation Unit, which will be headed by the Froject Coordinator and will have
four key staff, all with terms of reference and experience satisfactory to the Association.
3.17         Project implementation would begin in FY91/92, with priority given to the studies
(consultancy services) for: (a) source works (bulk supply development), in order to gain time as
the other engineering studies depend to a large extent on the results and recommendations of this
study; (ii) hydrogeological studies for the augmentation works in order to carry out the field
investigations before the commencement of the rainy season; and (iii) coordination/lia.son/
management consultancy/advisory services, to provide assistance to NWCPC in project
management. To facilitate the early commencement of these activities, the Bank has approved a
PPF advance of US$750,000 (No.P-647-0-KE) on March 29, 1991 for partial financing of these
services prior to the effectiveness of the proposed Credit. The Project implementation schedule is
shown in Annex 3.

3.19         Procurement of the major civil works and supply contracts for the
rehabilitation/augmentation works, exceeding US$0.5 million equivalent would be on the basis of
international competitive bidding (ICB). Vehicles and major n.intenance equipment and
machinery would be combined into supply contracts and would be procured through ICB.
Contracts for workshop equipments, minor machineries, tools and spare parts to cost less than the
equivalent of US$200,000 each up to an aggregate value of US$500,000 equivalent may be
awarded on the basis of local competitive bidding (LCB). Minor civil works contracts for the
rehabilitation/augmentation works, test drilling and building works, to cost less than the
equivalent of US$200,000 each, up to an aggregate amount of US$1.2 million may be awarded on
the basis of LCB. LCB procedures shall be satisfactory to the Association. Foreign bidders who
wish to participate will not be precluded from participation and no special preference will be
accorded to domestic contractors in case of foreign bidders participation. Contracts for goods
(office technology and training materials and equipment) estimated to cost less than the equivalent
of US$20,000 each up to an aggregate amount not to exceed of US$100,000 may be procured
under contracts awarded on the basis of local shopping or contracting. All contracts with a value
above US$100,000 equivalent should be subject to review by the Association. Total value of
individual contracts above US$100,000 equivalent is estimated at about 96% of the total value of
contracts.
3.20         Consultancy studies and engineering will be carried out by consultants whose
qualification, experience and terms and conditions of employment shall be satisfactory to the
Association. Such consultants shall be selected in accordance with the principles and procedures
satisfactory to the Association on the basis of the "Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by
World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency" published by the Bank in
August 1981. NWCPC requested the Bank that for the hydrological investigations near Baricho
and for the engineering and construction supervision of the rehabilitation/augmentation of
Baricho/Sabaki system Messrs. H.P. Gauff consulting engineers be retained for a total estimated
cost of about US$1.4 million. This request appears to be justified. The proposed engineering
consultancy is an extension/continuation of their current activities which include: (a) hydrological
and hydrogeological investigations for additional sources for the Baricho/Sabaki bulk water supply
syst.em; (b) design and construction supervision of a new wellfield on the right bank of hie Sabaki
river; (c) engineering studies for the improvement/modification of the existing river-intake
structure at Baricho (including a hydraulic model study); and (d) design and construction
supervision of the extension of the existing treated water pumping station at Baricho, and the
transmission main and distribution network for Malindi's water supply. Gauff's services have
been financed by MWD and KfW (US$0.5 m and 2.0 m, respectively) and their present
assignment is expected to be completed by end-1992. The total value of the physical works are
estimated at US$20 million (under KfW financing). Gauff's performance has been satisfactory
and their presence and intirrmate knowledge of the system are expected to reduce the required time
and cost for carrying out the consultancy for the additional augmentation/rehabilitation of the
system under the proposed project. In any event, Gauff will be requested to provide an offer
against specific termns of reference for this purpose and the contract will be awarded after clear;ng
verification of reasonability of price.

Environmental Aspects
3.25         The proposed engineering and rehabilitation Project will be subjected to a limited
environmental assessment in line with its categorization as an EC/EB project. The EB category
applies to the rehabilitation/augmentation component, with special regard to the development of a
new wellfield near Baricho, on the left banklflood plain of the Sabaki river. No involuntary
resettlement is required. The environmental assessment will be carried out as part of the
consultancy assignment for the preparation of the rehabilitation/augmentation works, and an
environmental action plan will be prepared, as appropriate, and will be subject to the approval of
NWCPC and the Bank prior to the commencement of the physical works. The draft TOR for this
study are attached in Annex 10, Part A. During negotiations agreement was reached that the



- 23 -
approval of the environmental action program would be a condition for disbursement for the
rehabilitation/augmentation (physical works) of the Sabaki/Baricho bulk water supply system.
3.26         The follow-on main investmenit project will require a full environmental impact
assessment in line with the World Bank Operational Directive 4.00, Annex A: Environmental
Assessment and Annex B: Environmental Policy tor Dam and Reservoir Projects, and pertinent
Operational Directives, Policy notes and Guidelines. During preappraisal, the mission assisted
NWCPC in preparing TORs for the environmental impact studies to be carried out as part of the
consultancy assignment for the source works (bulk supply) development (a) for the increased
utilization of the Mzima Springs source in the Tsavo West National Park, and (b) for other
selected sources, with special regard to the possibility of reservoir constructions (Annex 10,
Part B and C).
3.27         During the preappraisal mission, agreement was reached that an Environmental
Advisory Panel (EAP) will be set up with participation of NWCPC and government institutions
with specific environmental responsibilities in the country. Non-governmental environmental
organizations will also be kept informed and consulted regarding project plans and activities on a
regular basis and will be asked to participate in the EAP meetings, as appropriate. The Kenya
Wildlife Service and the Environmental Secretariat also endorsed the establishment of the EAP.
Affordability for Low Income Group
3.28         The water tariff in the low consumption bracket, which was adjusted upward from
9 m3/month to 10 m3/month, in accordance with the new water tariffs effective as of October 1,
1991, is a flat rate of KSh 30.0 per month. Low income household minimum monthly water
consumption in Mombasa is estimated as below:
20 I.p.c.d. x 5 persons per household x 30 days = 3000 1 or 3 m3/month
This being below the 10 m3/month consumption threshhold, the monthly cost for water for a low
income household with water connection would be KSh 30.0. At the kiosk rate of KSh 5.0 per
M3, the minimum quantity of 3 m3/month would cost KSh 15.0.
3.29         The median of the low income bracket for consumer price index, i.e. KSh 1,350
in 1987 prices (without adjustment for inflation for the period 1987 - 1990) was used as a proxy
for average monthly low household income. The expenditure for water for a household with
water connection would require about 2.2 percent of household income (or about 1.1 percent at
the kiosk rate).
3.30         An alternative approximation of household income is to use the actual nominal
monthly earning of a typical, modern sector unskilled laborer in manufacturing, i.e. about KSh
1,900. Assuming 1.5 wage earner per household, household income would be about KSh 2,850
per month. The water expenditure would in this case represent only about 1 percent of the
household income.
3.31         Based on above assumptions and calculations, there is room to increase tariffs by
over 100 percent without putting too high a burden on the low income group, as expenditure for
water would still be in the range of 2.0 - 4.5 percent range of income, which is within the
generally acceptable level of 3-5 percent for low income households.



- 24 -
3.32          A large portion of !)w income families, however, pay at present significantly
more for water, if bought from vendors, who quite often sell water at 5 to 6 times the official
kiosk retail price. This brings the percentage of income spent on water (20 l.p.c.d.) to 5.0 - 6.0
percent and the impact of further tariff increases on this group warrants special attention (para.
3.34).
3.33          While it is clear that substantial average tariff increases will be required over the
medium term (paras 5.04 through 5.07) to ensure NWCPC's financial viability, measures would
be taken to protect the interest of the lowest income groups through appropriately designed tariff
structure (para 5.08).
3.34          In addition to a more differentiated tariff structure, the proposed Project and the
follow-on main investment project would help the lowest income group by reducing the water
shortage and providing more equitable supply in an extended system, thereby reducing the need
for buying water at exorbitant prices from vendors. Attention would also be given to increasing
the number of water selling kiosks at strategic locations as part of the distribution network
improvement measures, and forming of women's associations as water retailers (kiosk owners)
would be encouraged. These associations already operate in Mombasa, utilizing their income
from water sales for various neighborhood development projects.



- 25 -
IV. THJ      LNA       W    CONSERVATION AND PIPELINE CORPORATION
Background iA% (_ranL4i a
4.01         The National Water Conservation and Pipeline Corporation (NWCPC) is a
corporate body established under the State Corporation Act by Legal Notice No. 270 of June 24,
1988. After setting up its headquarters and initial management systems, NWCPC effectively
began operations on July 1, 1989. Its purpose is to manage and develop water projects
specifically entrusted to it, including the supply of bulk and retail water within those project
areas, on a commercially oriented basis. NWCPC Is responsible for the operation and
implementation of 45 water schemes, both urban and regional in nature, taken over from the
Ministry of Water Development (MWD). This includes those schemes related to the Mombasa
and Coastal Region, as well as those expected to be revenue-earning operations for smaller urban
and rural areas 1/. Its mandate also includes the responsibility to assist the Government in the
formulation and execution of a national water policy.
4.02          NWCPC reports to MWD and operates under a Board consisting of
representatives from various concerned government ministries as well as the private sector. Its
chief executive officer, the Managing Director, is appointed by the President and acts as
Chairman of the Board. While NWCPC is an autonomous organization, the Ministry of Finance
is expected to play a key role in reviewing NWCPC's borrowing requirements and in acquiring
and guaranteeing loans for NWCPC. In addition, according to present practice, water tariff
increases have to be approved by the Cabinet.
4.03          NWCPC's organizational structure consists of four departments, each headed by a
Chief. The departments and divisions are the following:
-      Finance and Hum.an R-ourCes Department with an Accouuiing and a
Human Resources Division.
-      Operations Department with an Operations Division (supervising the
Regional Offices) and a Technical Support Division.
-      Development Services Department (responsible for standards development
and monitoring, designs and construction).
-      Corporation Services Department organized into three divisions for
Planning, Scientific Services and Information Technology.
In addition, a Corporation Secretary, responsible for legal matters and Board and Committee
services, reports to the Managing Director. NWCPC's present organization structure is well
suited to its needs and provides a sound framework for future growth. The organization chart in
Annex 7 shows the tasks and responsibilities of the various departments and divisions. The
establishment of a Project Implementation Unit (PIU) in a strong central position, as a permanent
If The locations of the schemes are shown on IBRD Map 23240 and the main features of the
major schemes (traditionally named after the location of the source works and not the
supply area) are tabulated in Annex 6.



- 26 -
part of the organization is Justified, due to NWCPC's inexperience in implementing LIrge, multi-
laterally financed projects and the magnitude of expected future investments. The organizational
structure for the Mombasa and Coast Region, NWCPC's largest cost center, consists of four
divisions for administration, finance, engineering and operations and maintenance, each headed by
a division chief who report to the Regional Manager (Annex 8).
Staffing, Manpower Develonment and Training
4.04          NWCPC's total staff currently (as of July 1, 1991) is about 1,200, decreased from
about 1,700 during the previous year in order to promote efficiency in operations and reduce
unnecessary costs. The majority of the staff were initially seconded from MWD but are now
retained by NWCPC. Therefore, while NWCPC is still a fledging institution, it enjoys the
advantage that most of its professional staff were previously involved in the management and
operation of the water schemes it inherited.
4.05          The number of staff in NWCPC's headquarters and in the operational regions is
shown in Table 4.1.

headquarters reflects the strong centralized management of NWCPC which is necessary for a
newly-created institution. The staffing by regions are generally adequate for the present
operations of NWCPC, except in the Coastal Region where the percentage of qualified personnel
appears to be low and may need to be increased with the extension of the system. The adequacy
of staffing at regional levels and for individual schemes is reviewed and adjusted from time to
time, especially as the rehabilitation, extension or upgrading of serviced levels of individual
schemes take place.

4.06          NWCPC has prepared adequately detailed job descriptions for each position
s5hown in the organMization chart. Twvo senior executive posts (Chief Corporate Services Manager
and Chief Development Services Manager) are presently vacant and the functions are carried out
by acting assignments. Recruitment efforts are presently under way to fill these positions,
together with some vacancies at mid-management level. NWCPC's salary levels and benefits are
reasonably competitive with the private sector, ensuring a wide market for recruitment efforts.
4.07          NWCPC as a new and growing entity has a variety of requirements for the
training of its new staff as well as for the staff transferred to it from otlher government agencies to
develop professional competencies in line with the objectives and responsibilities of a
commercially- oriented organization that was not required of them under their earlier, civil
service roles. The need to assess NWCPC's training requirements and to develop a systematic
approach to address these requirements has been fully recognized by NWCPC's management.
4.08          As a first step to formulate an appropriate training policy and to facilitate the
preparation of a training program to be supported under the proposal Project, NWCPC prepared
an in-house study for discussion with the appraisal mission. The salient points of this study are
the following:
(a)    NWCPC appropriately decided to employ a full time training officer
(whoce scope of work is well definued in the study);



- 28 -
(b)   detailed assessment of NWCPC's training requirement is a prerequisite for
the formulation of an appropriate training policy;
(c)   training should initially focus on the following areas: public utilities
management, financial management, accounting and auditing, and water
resources management. Attention should also be given to improved
computer literacy and to the training of technicians and plant operators.
4.09         The study also emphasizes the use of training facilities in Kenya, as the most cost-
effective way of training. Beside formal (classroom) training (basic and refresher courses), it is
proposed that priority should be given to in-house and on-the-job training which can be tailored to
NWCPC's most pressing needs. The Project would provide funding for training equipment and
materials to support NWCPC's effort in developing and providing in-house training facilities.
4.10         Training activities in Kenya should be supplemented by overseas training,
especially at higher and mid-management levels. To facilitate overseas training, the Project
would provide for the financing of post-graduate and diploma courses in engineering,
accountancy, and computer studies and participation in selected workshops, seminars and short
courses in engineering, water resources management, financial management, and management and
administration of water utilities, including visits and short-term exchange programs with selected
water authorities abroad.
4.11         An outline training program has been developed jointly with NWCPC during
appraisal (Annex 14). During negotiations agreement was reached that NWCPC would submit a
detailed training program for each fiscal year for the period FY92/93 through FY95/96
satisfactory to the Association as a condition for disbursement for the training component for each
year.
Corporate Development Plan
4.12         A necessary prerequisite for establishing proper objectives and goals over the
medium term for NWCPC, which has just started the transition from government to commercial
practices and policies, will be the preparation of a 5-year Corporate Development Plan, which
would be supported under the Project. The Corporate Development Plan will be expected to
identify the priority activities, policies and operational and financial targets to be achieved as well
as provide recommendations on the proposed management organization required to carry out the
plan. An important feature of the plan will be the preparation of a specific 5-year investment and
financing plan which considers the Corporation's requirements for maintenance and operations as
well as the likely revenues and requirements for outside borrowings. Included in the consultancy
to assist NWCPC to prepare the Corporate Plan will be a requirement to provide
recommendations for developing a corporate planning capability within NWCPC.
4.13         Since the inception of NWCPC, many of the responsibilities for water sector
operations, management and policy formulation which previously fell under the domain of MWD
and municipalities are now shared with the Corporation. In order to provide a framework for
identifying needed strategies and policies and a clearer delineation of responsibilities for NWCPC,
it will also be necessary to undertake a background study of the water sector. This proposed
water sector study is included in the consultancy to prepare the Corporate Development Plan.
The terms of reference for the consultancy for the Corporate Development Plan, including a tariff
study, were agreed during appraisal and are presented in Annex 9. The Corporate Development



- 29 -
Plan is scheduled to be prepared by end-December 1993. During negotiations, agreement was
reached that NWCPC would submit the Corporate Development Plan for review and comments by
the Association not later than December 31, 1993.
Accounts and Audits
4.14         NWCPC has already begun the process of shifting over from government
accounting to commercial accounting procedures. Financial statements, in a commercial format,
have been prepared by NWCPC for its first two years of operations in FY89/90-FY90/91, and
auditing its FY89/90 accounts was underway during project appraisal. The audit report for
NWCPC's FY89/90 which was submitted prior to negotiations was in line with the previously
reported unaudited accounts.
4.15         Project accounts would be prepared by NWCPC for each year during project
Implementation. The project accounts would identify all sources and uses of funds in carrying out
the Project, including a detailed accounting of the use of the proceeds of the IDA Credit and the
operation of the Special Account. The project accounts' audit reports would also contain a
separate opinion on the statement of expenditure (SOE) procedure, when utilized.
4.16         Tn order to monitor the flnancial performance of NWCPC, agreement was reached
during negotiat. ins that its financial statements (Income Statements, Sources and Applications of
Funds Statements and Balance Sheets) along with the project accounts would be audited by an
independent auditor acceptable to IDA and the reports would be submitted to IDA within nine
months of the end of the fiscal year. The period of nine months is considered reasonable, based
on the first year's experience whereby the audit was completed in about twelve months. Future
audits should be completed in a shorter period when NWCPC completes the installation of its own
computer system (para 4.21). The audit reports would contain a separate opinion on compliance
by NWCPC with all financial covenants under the IDA Credit and operation of the Special
Account. It is expected that the Controller and Auditor-General would either carry out the audits
or appoint private auditors to carry out the audits on its behalf.
Billing and Collection
4.17         Ihe current billing system for Mombasa and the Coastal Region is basically a
metere(A billing system, although the system now bills many consumers by estimated water
consumption due to faulty meters. It is currently estimated that about 40-50% of the area's
53,000 meters are malfunctioning. To address this shortcoming, the proposed Project will
provide funds to replace about 25,000 meters over a 2-year period.
4.18         Meter readings are carried out on a monthly basis by the area's meter readers
(about 60) and sent in batches to the Government Computer Center in Nairobi for processing of
bills. Where meters are far apart as in some rural areas, the readings i.ake longer than one month
and are a cause of delay, since the billing cycle cannot presently be closed unless all the meter
books are entered. Therefore, billings are currently behind by about 2 months. Payments of bills
are received at Mombasa Headquarters and sub-area offices (7) in the region. Funds collected are
initially banked in Mombasa until all accounts are reconciled and then transferred to NWCPC's
main account in Nairobi. Disconnections are undertaken in principle for consumers who are 45
days late in payments but in practice are undertaken on a sub-area basis, causing disconnections to
be up to six months late. Currently, it is difficult to quickly locate consumer Information to
verify bills paid and as a result, some consumers who have paid are disconnected.



- 30 -
4.19         Examination of NWCPC's income statements and customer accounts for FY89190
and FY90191 for the Mombasa and Coastal Region shows that there was substantial overbilling of
water revenues. The quantity of water billed was about twice the amount estimated to be
available with the result that the average calculated tariff of KSh 10.5/r3 was about double the
average official tariff of about Ksh 5/rn (see para 5.05). The primary factor contributing to
overbilling is considered to be the continuation of billing of accounts which have been closed.
Bills are currently sent to tenants, not landlords, of the properties supplied with water. Due to
the delays in the billing cycle, the final bill for an account which has been closed is produced
about three months after the consumer has vacated the property with the effect that the bill
becomes impossible to collect. Since the closed accounts for these customers show outstanding
balances due, the current computer billing program continues to produce bills for the minimum
amount. To address this problem, NWCPC staff have recently begun verifying accounts which
have been closed and are modifying the computer program to discontinue billing of these
accounts. In addition, payment of all future accounts must now be guaranteed by landlords, and a
program of identifying landlords of prior customer accounts has been initiated. Another factor
contributing to overbilling occurs when meters are replaced which have different readings than the
old meters, but the readings are not properly recorded in the computer system in a timely manner.
4.20         In order to improve the billing and collection cycle, NWCPC must have correct
and up-to-date information of its consumers' accounts to permit accurate and timely action to be
taken. NWCPC has accorded high priority to improving the financial performance of the schemes
it inherited. With French Government assistance, NWCPC has embarked on a program of
verification of consumer accounts and follow-up, review of meter reading and billing procedures
along with replacement of meters on a pilot basis in Mombasa city (part of North Mainland).
Further assistance would be provided under the project to extend this pilot effort to all sections of
Mombasa and other urban areas within the Coastal Region and review the requirements for an
improved overall financial management and management information system. The terms of
reference for this consultancy were agreed during preappraisal and are presented in Annex 9.
The financial systems improvements are scheduled to be completed by end-December 1993.
4.21         In addition, NWCPC has already initiated a consultancy to develop a
computer billing system which will be operated at the regional office without reliance on the
Government Computer Center. The software is also being designed to permit transactions to
be entered on a continuous rather than on a batch basis, which will contribute to shortening
the billing cycle. The first phase of this consultancy has been completed, and the Project
would provide for the completion and installation of the software as well as for the hardware
requirements, which are expected to be completed by end-June 1992. To provide further
support for revenue collection, meter replacement and disconnections, additional vehicles and
motorcycles would be provided under the Project to better serve the various sub-areas.
