ANNEX I
THE MOMBASA AND COASTAL WATER SUPPLY SYSTEM
Iltroductlon
The Mombasa and Coastal Water Supplies consists of a complex of rural and
urban water supplies in Killfi, Kwale, Taita Taveta, Lamu and Mombasa Districts.
Very few schemes have independent sources as the entire system is an
interlinked and extensive netvwork of major pipelines of Mzima, Sabaki, Marere
and Malindi being the most prominent.
1.1   Mombasa Distribution System
The distribution system in Mombasa was conceived to take an ultimate
capacity of 43,000 m3/day this being the capacity for Mzima and Marere
sources. The commissioning of Sabaki pipeline in 1981 brought an extra
35,000 m3iday without the corresponding upgrading of the distribution
system although it had been intended to do so. In order to satisfy
Mombasa's immediate water demands in the short term, It is necessary
to optimise the performance of the existing distribution system including
rehabilitation, augmentation and reduction in unaccounted-for-water.
1.2  South-Coast Distributlon System
This system is provided with water from Marere springs and the Tiwi
groundwater boreholes. These boreholes produce about 4,OOOm3/day.
There is major cievelopment in tourism along tne South Coast stretching
from Shelly Beach to Diani Beach. The existing distribuTion system
particularly in Diani is hardly adequate to cater for the current demand.
There is also a high density residential area of Likoni which Is served by
old distribution system again needing rehabilitation.
&.3  Kwale Distributlon System
Kwale is one of the major urban centres served from Marare source works
and needs upgrading of the distribution.
1.4  Baricho Distribution System
Malindi, Watamu, and Kilifi form the main distribution system of Baricho.
While Malindi distribution is currently undergoing upgrading, there is need
to rehabilitate and augment distribution systems in other areas including
off-takes enroute to Mombasa.
2.    Present Water Production
Mombasa receives the bulk of water produced from the sources. However, the
system in Mombasa and the environs is characterised by low pressures in the
distribution system.



Annex 10
-112-                       Page 25 of 37
2.1   Marere Pipeline
This system was developed In 1923 and has a maximum production of
12,000 m3/day. Most of the water Is consumed enroute such that about
4,000 m3/day gets into Changamwe reservoirs.
2.2   Mzilma Pipellne
This is a 220 km long pipeline built in early 1950's with an expected outr
of about 40,000 m3/day although only about 32,000 m3/day gets to
Mazeras and Is then further reducea by off-takes before getting to
Mombasa. There has been consistent breakdowns due to bursts
particularly on sectlons w;in black cotton soil resulting In shut-downs,
hence intermittent supply.
2.3   Sabakl Pipeline
This is the main pipeline serving the North of Mob-nbasa inclusive of
Malindi, Kilifi, and North Coast. Only about 35,000 m3/day gets to Nguu
Tatu reservoir for distribution to North Coast and ultimately to Mombasa.
Continued breakdown In the pumping installations hes also resulted in
reduced prod6jotion levels.
2.4   Tiwl Boreholes
These produce about 4,000 m3/day which is fully utillsed in SoUth Coast.
-.i   Distribution Systems
Water is distributed to consumers through individual meters and water
retailer kiosks. The latter are used in high density areas. Although
distribution network pipeline drawings are available at the rsgional level,
they are not up to date and relate to early 1970's except newer mains laid
In M&Iindi. Extensions on smaller distribution mains done later area  t
shown on the drawings. The information on statws of the existing
distribution mains is also scanty. However, most of the mains in the main
distribution areas of Mombasa Municipality are A.C. However, ductile
Iron, PVC and steel pipes form also part of the distributions. Consultants
should take notice of this as there will be need to locate such pipes where
not shown on the networks.
It Is expected that aerial survey block-mapping, and network analysis
would be carried out under a separate works programme concurrently In
order to update the drawings and render implemeirntation of an
appropriate leak detection and repair policy operable.
2.6   Metering
Production figures are not reliable as the meters are generally out of



Annex 10
-113-                        Page 26 of 37
order. Consumers are metered through consumer meters of sizes
150mm to 15mm. However, a high percentage of consumer meters (at
least 30%) are out of order while an unknown percentage are
under-registering. Most of the major consumer meters are out of order
resulting in estimated consumption. Consequently, while billing system
is metered the accuracy of water sold is questionable. Hence with
unreliable production figures and water sold data on the unaccounted for
water is unreliable. No bulk zonal meters are installed at the moment
within the distribution systems.

C.
SECOND MOMBASA AND COASTAL WATER SUPPLY
ENGINEERING AND REHABILITATION PROJECT
TERMS OF REFERENCE
FOR CONSULTANCY SERVICES FOR THE
REHABILITATION/AUGMENTATION OF THE SABAKI
BULK WATER SUPPLY SCHEME
(SABAKI WATER PROJECT)
The Sabaki Water Project was originally put forwi.rd as one
of a number of alternatives for consideration in the early
1970's when the then project consultants produced a report
on the results of their "investigations into an additional
.source to provi%.X the necessary immediate augmentation of
the Mombasa Pipeline Board Supplies". The terms of
reference required that a water source be found to provide
sufficient additional water to meet expanding demand in
MoMbasa up until 1990.
This, it was estimated would require augmentation by about
55,000m3/day; however initially and on the basis of
economic merit the report recommended a scheme based upon
the Pemba river yielding approximately, 32,000 m3/day with
the alternatives at the higher supply figure, in order of
their preference, being schemes based upon the Sabaki
river, the Mwachi river or a second pipeline from Mzima
Springs.
The Client decided that the scope of the original study
should be widened to consider and compare methods by which
water supplies could be augmented and water supplied not
only to Mombasa but also the entire coastal region north of
Mombasa.
In particular the consultants were asked to investigate
further a scheme for the development of the Sabaki river
and as a result submitted a supplementary report in July
1972.
In the supplementary report, three alternative schemes were
considered, two of which were based upon a development of
the Sabaki river while the third was based on a further
development of Mzima Springs. It was concluded that for the
1990 supply to the entire area, estimated at 72,000 m3/day,
a schem based upon the Sabaki river was most suitable.
Studies for possible intake sites concluded that the most
promising river reach was on a lOkm section of the Sabaki

river between Chakama ard Baricho, whilst the Baricho area
also offered the possibility of a dam site with a reservoir
storage potential of over 1100 million cubic metres.
In discussing the alternative sites, recommendations were
made for a full site investigation, initially at Baricho.
It was indicated that there would be need for some form of
low impounding structure behind which water would be stored
to enoure that, regardless of how the low flow channels
changed course, there would always be an adequate depth of
water adjacent to the intake structure.
It was recognised that the provision of any type of fixed
impounding barrier across the Sabaki would involve
overcoming two major problems. In the first place the pond
behind the barrier would rapidly silt up during floods and
secondly the scour problems downstream of any fixed
structure were considered to be formidable.
The possibility of utilising an inflatable weir that would
be raised during low flow periods and deflated during
floods was suggested.
An a result of the supplementary report, the consultants
were instructed in May 1973 to proceed with the preliminary
design of the Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Project wwith
the Sabaki river as the source.
The resulting preliminary design report proposed the
construction of:-
i)   aan intake on the Sabaki river about 40 km west of
Malindi; near the village of Baricho;
ii)   a raw water pumping station at the intake site;
iii)   a modern water treatment plant, including
pre-settlement tanks to remove the heavier silt
concentration during periods of flood flows in the
river;
iv)   a treated water pumping station;
v)   a main transmission pipeline to the existing service
reservoir installation at Changamwe in the West
Maidand district of Mombasa;
vi)   two booster pumping stations on the main transmisuion
pipeline;-

A two stage project was envisaged with stage I being
capable of supplying 54,000 m3/day to be expanded betwsan
1980 and 1984 to an ultimate amount of 72,000 m3/day of
which 67,400 could be pumped towards tdombasa , sufficient
for the period up to 1990.
Before finally deciding on the intake site, four were
initially considered. The requirements at preliminary
design stage were seen as a site where water could be
abstracted at all times whatever the prevailing conditions
of the river; a site where the channel and valley widths
were minimal ar;d the channel form and alignments favourable
to the operation of an intake, and where rooks at shallow
depth on the intake/raw water pumphouse site would offer
secure and simple foundations adjacent to and a steep
inerodible hillside that would facilitate thhe provision of
all weather access to the pumphouse and would rise well
clear of all flood water.
The site termed the 'lower Baricho site', 62 km from the
sea was confirmed as most suitable being locited just
downstream og the Lango Baya fault scarp where t.he river
entered a confined reach where the inerodable valley sides
were only 250 m to 300m apart and the hill slopes
relatively steep.
The position for the proposed intake was on the outside of
the shallow southward-facing bend at a point where the
river impinged against a hard bank at the: foot of a steep
hillside.
The report continued, "the raw water pumphouse will be
located on the edge of the right bank where the foundations
will be on ro6k. It will have a short bunded access raised
to a level well above the maximum design flood level.
xediately adjacent a short intake structure will feed
water from the river to the pumphouse sump.
"The control structure is at a point on the river where the
required regulation of water-levels can be simply achieved,
where the founding and construction of the control weir and



Annex 10
-117-                       Page 30 of 37
ancilla.-y structures can be econonaically achieved and where
it seems there will be no particular constvaction problems.
River training, if needed, will require only simple stone
spurs. Normal or catastrophic flood flows and levels will
neither restrict the operation of the intake nor lead to
such conditions as might endanger its immediate security or
restrict its useful life.
"It also has the long term advantage of being closely
adjacent to and downstream of a potential impounding dam
site although the feasibility and need for this has yet to
be proved".
The control structure  proposed was now to comprise " a low
fixed-river structure lying in the bed of the channel and
contained between training walls on either bank. It will
have a downstream apron to contain the standing wave which
will form at low and medium flows and the whole will be
contained within sheet piling driven into the bed.
"The crest of the weir will be horizontal and continous
except on the right bank where there will be a small gat;ed
flushing sluice for flow regulation and local sediment
control. The gate will be mounted on a frame which can be
swung back against the adjacent training wall and clear of
the uncontrolled flood flows and thu associated debris
which might otherwise accumulate and foul the intake
imediately upstream". No reference to a inflatable weir an
previously put forward was repeated.
The proposal at that time for the raw water pumping station
and intake envisaged using equal sized submersible pumps or
possibly vertical spindle wet well pumps fed from an intake
capable of operating over the full raange of flows and at
all times avoiding "drawing in water heavily laden with
silt if better is available".
The form of the intake openings was evolved from
discussions held with the Hydraulics Research Station,
Wallingford, england, the intake being designed to draw in
the best available water and "by smooth flow and low
threshold velocities, discourage the entry of the coaaer
sediments which adversely affect pump wear and add to the
sediment load carried through to the treatment works.
The prelimhinary design for the intake, control structure
and training works was submitted to the Hydraulics Research
Station (HRS), Wallingford, UK for testing by means of a
hydraulic model.
Following field surveys and design modifications, the model

entrance and the floww control sluice gate.
c) If the deep water =hannel remains near the south bank,
the entrance to the intake channel should be
satisfactory for most floods. If it moves away and large
shoals move downstream then spurs to scout the bed near
the channel entrance should be constructed.
d) The lower intakes should be used for the extraction of
water when the level is lesso than 51.0 m above datum.
Above thia level the lower intakes should be closed and
the upper intakes used.

On rising stage it may be opened at a stage of 50.4 m

basin will be flushed away at high discharge provided
the deep water channel remains near the south bank.
h) Large floods may cause accretion in and near the intake
channel. Under such circumstances sediment will have to
b2 removied mechanically.

During the initial preparatory stage for the Baricho Intake
Study, a topographically attractive off-river site some 7
km. upstream of 1oaricho near Chakama and on the south bank
had been noted from the 1:50,000 topographical maps. A
subsequent study of the relevant aerial photographs made
the site look more attractive, sufficient to justify both
an initial survey and to warrant a visit from the Study
Geologist.
A 20 metre high dam, 110 - 150 m long, would be practicable
and provides a storage volume of about 27 mio. cu. metres.
Because of the very small catchment, a small flood ou-..flow
unit would be all that was required. The geology of the dam
site is also promising being a steep sided quartzite gorge,
with coarse grained Mazeras sandstone sides.
Material for a rockfill dam could probably be won from a
quarry in the gorge itself.
At reconnaissance level, there would appear to be two ways
of filling and utilising such storage.
(a)   Gravity Inflow (Dual Purpose)
The 1:50,000 topographical maps indicate that the
gradient of the Sabaki river some 30 - 35 km.
upstream of Baricho has a short section somewhat
steeper than average gradient.
It would seem practicable to take advantage of this
to divert by gravity into a reservoir in the
off-river Chakama site.
A side channel intake with gravity pipeline and
tunnel or deep cut channel into the Chakama reservoir
and onward gravity flow to the Baricho Intake,
therefore appears possible, but is subject to
confirmatory field survey, in relation to intake
levels.
By appropriate sizing of intake and gravity inflow
pipe, it would be practicable to utilise the
reservoir both as a low-flow augmentation reservoir
and a; an indirect water source during periods of
high flow. In this way, no abstraction either
directly at Baricho or at the upstream intake would
be made during high-flow, high-suspended load periods
and instead at such times water would be drawn from
the Chakama reservoir.
(b)   Pumped Inf low (Single Purpose)
In terms of utilising suchi storage for the single
purpose of augmenting dry weather flow, an



Annex 1 0
-124-
Page 37 of 37
alternative would be to install low-lift pumps at
13aricho and to pump water up into the Chakama
reservoir during periods of moderate river flow to be
able to draw from such storage during periods when
the natural river flow was insufficient. The pipeline
between Baricho and the Chakama reservoir would serve
as both the rising main and the return flow gravity
main.
Such an option would have a significantly lower
capital cost than option (a), but higher operating
costs. It could be used to both augment dry weather
and avoid direct abstraction of high sediment located
water during flood, but would be more practicable as
a single purpose low-ilow augmentation scheme.

A.
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR
AN ENVIRONMMENTAL IMPACT ASSESSEMENT OF TlE PROPOSED
SECOND MOM BASA AND COASTAL WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
(Applicable for all source works development alternatives)
Introduction
1.   The purpose of this terms of reference is to guide and structure the
preparation of an environmental impact assessment of the Second Mombasa
Water Supply Project.
2.   The development project to be assessed Is presently not yet specffied as to
the fnal technical afternative to be developed. However, the situation leading
up to the present project consideratons and pre-planning are as follows: The
area of the Kenyan coast under review for augmented water supply through
the project includes primarily greater Mombasa (island and mainland), the
north coast up to Malindi, tie major development centres on the immediate
South coast, and the hinterland along this coastal strp. This area covers
approximately 1000 square kilometres and has presentty (1991) an estimated
populaton of nearly 1.7 million inhabitants.
3.   The estmated average daily demaid for fresh water in this area is around
200,000 cubio meters. The demand from the Mombasa urban centre and
surroundings, which is the focus of the present project proposal, counts for
nearly 125,000 cubic meters of the above amount. However, the pres; !'f total
average public supply is less than 85,000 cubic meters of water, leaving a
deficit that means strict water rationing wKth consequent restrictions on
development and human use. Projections for future water demand in the
Mombasa and surrounding area is estimated to almost 200,000 cubic meters
daily by the year 2000.
4.    The present public sLupply is provided from four sources;
Mzima Springs in Tsavo National Park  approx  35,000 cu.m
Marere Springs/Pemba River           U        9,000 cu.m
South Coast (Tiwi) boreholes                  4,000 cu.rn
Baricho treatment works on Sabaki River      35,000 cu.m
5.    The Baricho treatment works operates at 2/3 capacity while the rest works
at full capaciy. The Baricho treatment works has a very high operating cost
while the Mzima Springs has very low operating costs. Mzima Springs
provides gravity fed clean spring water while Baricho cleans and treat
extremely silt-polluted water from the river and pumps it to the consumers.
A solution to the unsatisfactory water situation and provision of adequat.9
fresh water supplies to satisfy the demand in Mombasa and surroundings is
considered the highest political and development priority at the moment.
6.    Responsibility for the executing arrangements for the environmental
assessment rests with Kenya Government as potential borrower of project
funds from the World Bank. The National Water Conservation and Pipeline



Annex I 1
-126-                       Page 2 of 38
Corporation (NWCPC) represents the Kenyan Government on technical
project matters and will be the contracting partner for the consultant
undertaking the Impact assessment. The World Bank's guidelines for
environmenta impact assessments will apply.
Background Information
7.   The major components of the proposed project have not yet been agreed
upon. The specific requirements of the assessment will therefore be
formulated at a later stage.
A first phase of the project consisting of the rehabilitation of the old system,
InstiUon building, and the preparations for the main investment is dealt with
as a separate project and is presently under appraisal. This limited proJect
was initially classified a C-category according to the World Bank's
Operational Directive 4.00 (needing no assessment), while the second phase
was expected to be a category A, needing full assessment. However, the first
phase project has a part (part II: Rehabilitaton and augmentation works);
(see below) which is a category B component needing llmited assessment

Review and update of existing feasibility studies with special
regard to the proposed Mzima Springs Project, Including

environmental impact studies and preliminary engineering
for the selected alternative.

- Preparation of studies, detailed engineering designs, bid
documents and evaluation of bids for all components of the
Second Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Project main
investment phase (phase 2), (excluding project
supervision) that is for:
- the selected alternative for source work development.
- water treatment.
- transmission mains with all ancillary structures (access
roads)
- terminal reservoirs
- distribution systems in Mombasa and other coastal centres.

development near Baricho.
- Consultancy services for engineering designs, bid
documents and evaluation.
- Sabaki Intake Model study.
10.  Total project cost for all the above components of the first project phase is
estimated to be US$ 40.00 million.
The major Investment project to follow is not defined, but is expected to have
a cost of an estimatedl US$ 200.00 million.
11.  The need for this project is without question and the objectives it is intended
to meet is to provide relief for the population from the precarious water
situation in the project area. The urgent need for adequate and clean
freshwater supplies to the population and for coastal development should be
met without unnecessary delays, but the urgency must not lead to hasty and
wrong decisions causing or leading to long-term negative or damaging
external impacts affecting the sustainability of the natural resource base of
the region.
12.  The implementing agency is the National Conservation and Pipeline
Corporation (NWCPC). The Corporation and its institutional systems and
operations are also in need of rehabilitation and strengthening. This is



Annex 1 1
=.12
-128-                         Page 4 of 38
planned as an integral part of the first phase of the project.
13.  Consultants were already In the early 1970's Investigating additional sources
for the augmentation of the "Mombasa Pipeline Board Supplies", seeking
altornatives to provide sufficientaddftional water to meet expanding demands
In Mombasa up to 1990. The client later decided to Increase the scope of the
study to Incorporate not only supplies to Mombasa but to the entire coastal
region north of Mombasa. Three alternative schemes were identified, two of
which were based upon development of the Sabaki River while the third was
based on further development of the Mzima Springs. (The existing Mzima
pipeline dates back to the early 1950's.) One of the Sabaki River alternatives
was preferred and implemented, but the project has never reached the
capacity originally foreseen for several reasons.
14.  A Japanese funded study of 20 identified alternatives for water supply to the
coastal region was finalized in 1981. This study concluded in the end that
water tfom the Sabaki River led into a storage reservoir at the Rare River and
from there to Mombasa after treatment was the best alternative, with Mzlma
11 and a regulating dam on the lower Tsavo river as the second best
alternative.
15.   Due .o various delays leading to the present critical water supply situation
on the coast which gives serious urgency to a solution, a second pipeline
from Mzilma Springs together with various rehabilitation works are now being
considered as the easiest to realize with the lowest possible operational costs
(A separate Terms of Reference for this alternative has been elaborated).
However, recent discoveries of potentially rich water bearing layers in the
subterranean old plaeo channel of the Sabaki River now gives rise to
optimism as to the potential for significant borehole production in the close
proxImy to Baricho. Other dam- or impoundment solutions may also be
considered.
16.  Current status and timetable of the proposed project; Appraisal of the first
rehabilitation and institution building phase together with the engineering
credit facility is expected to take place in June/July 1991. The successful
negotiation of this component could release funding of the US$ 40.00 million
package by early 1992. The main component phase, still to be decided, could
have a partly overlapping parallel development leading to its possible
conclusion by thA latter part of the 1990's.
17.  There are none associated projects planned or in progress within the region
which may compete for the same resources, although private development
of local waterworks along the coast is a common result of the present
situation. Particularly the large tourist hotels and certain industrial complexes
are desperate to get adequate water supplies and are seeking alternative
solutions. It is not expected that this will significantly affect the proposed
project.
Objectives of the Environmental Impact Assessment.
18.  The main focus of the environmental assessment will be on the water
producing capacity of the selected source alternative, the pertinent



Annex 1 1
-129-                           Page 5 of 38
catchment area, and the effects on the downstream vegetation, wildlife,
ecology and aquder recharge of a reduced waterflow In the river systems as
a result of the project. It will also focus on the disturbance created by project
activtes on wildlie in the Tsavo National Park and the impacts on tourism
and the local population to the extent that may be signiflcant.
19.  Most of the activitles covered by the first project phase consisting of
rehablitation works and Institutional strengthening Is likely to have llmited
negative environmental Impacts. This has been anticipated by its
classffication as a C category project although this was later changed to a B
category. Since this project phase is likely to lead directly into, or follow
parallel to the preparation and design of the main project (second phase),
the environmental impact assessment should take place as soon as possible.
EnvIronmnental Anessment Requirements
20.  The regulations and guidelines which will govern the conduct of the
assessment include:
- World Bank Operational Directive 4.00, Annex A: Environmental
Assessment, and Annex B: Environmental Policy for Dam and Reservoir
Projects, and pertinent Operatonal Directives, Operational Policy Notes,
and Guidelines cited in Annex A2 thereto;
- National laws and/or regulations on environmental revlews and impact
assessments.
- Regional, provincial or communal environmental assessment regulations.
- Environmental assessment regulations of any other financing
organizations involved In the project.
Study Area
21.  Almost independent of which major second phase alternative is selected in
the end, the boundaries of the influence area to be studied by the assessment
incorporates in broad terms an area that includes the south eastern flanks
of Kilimanjaro within Kenya as the upper watershed of the Tsavo River, the
Chyulu Hills as catchment area for Mzima Springs and other springs to the
east of the Chyulus, Tsavo East and Tsavo West Nationai Parks and the area
between these hills, national parks and the sea, including the Immediate
offshore area. Particular attention will be focused on the rivers and springs
in this area, inciuding the Mzima River in its fuil length, the T savo River from
its upper reaches to the confluence with the Galana River, the Galana
(Sabaki) river from its confluence with the Tsavo River to the sea, the
immediate ofshore area near the mouth of the Sabaki River that includes the
coral reefs, the Voi and Rare rivers, Pemba river and the water distribution
areas The study area, include the areas used by wild animals that relates to
the river ecosystems and water supplies. The study area also includes a
broad belt of influence along pipelines and construction areas, related roads,
dams, reservoirs and impoundments, camps and activities. Other areas
adjacent to or influenced by the project should be considered with respect
to impacts of the project. Such direct or indirect environmental influences or
impacts from the project may include, for example, transportation corridors
(e.g. access to water works and facilities, treatment plants and facilities,



Annex I 1
-130-                          Page 6 of 38
distributon facilities, pipelines and related works). The indirect effects,
pardcularly the influx of population caused or facilitated by access roads and
project-related facilities, and the activities of that population including
settlement, agriculture, poaching, pollution, and fuel gathering, may be more
significant than the direct effects of the project.
Scope of Work
22.  The scope is primarily to analyze the environmental impacts of the project,
In particular impacts on the ecology, wildlife and vegetation that depends on
the watersheds tapped by the project. Attention must be paid to the rivers
and riverine ecology in the whole area Including impacts on beaches and
coral reefs off-shore. It will include impacts on tourism, the local population
in general terms, and the tourism economy, considering also the impact an
Inadequate water supply may have on that industry and the population i the
project Is not Implemented. Attention will also be given to altemative solutions
and water sources, and to the stability of the upper watersheds.
Umnology, hydrology and geomorphology with particular attention on the
sustainable water production, water quality and water balance of the
complete area should be investigated, and safe water off-take limits under
different climatc seasons should be estimated with specified probability
margins. Specialized field studies or modelling activities may have to be
carried out and the consuitants will be asked to define the specific tasks In
more detail for contracting agency review and approval.
CONSULTANT TASKS:
Task 1.    Description of the Proposed Project:
23.   Provide a full description of the project and its general setting; and as
applicable:
The proposed project: general location and layout; size of area involved in
total and in the various components (e.g., catchment area, drainage area,
pipeline area, reservoirs areas, ponds and dams, camps and settlements,
project roads, project related facilities, water distribution areas, other
construction activities involved, staff required and new communities and
other facilities involved; operation and maintenance; planned public or
private off-site investments as a result of the project; life span.
Task 2.    Baseline Data - Description of the Environment:
24.   Assemble, evaluate and present baseline data on the environmental
characteristic8 of the study area, using, as appropriate, maps, aerial
photography and satellite imagery. The hydrologic balance of the area
should be estimated. Where there are inadequate existing data, a full year of
observations (to define a full annual cycle) is required as a minimum, and a
mufti-year monitoring program established. The baseline data shall be
described in a way so as to be useful for long term monitoring and
comparison.
Physical environment:
- Geology and geomorphology, seismicity and stability of the area.

should be established as early as poss!ble to help ensure that pro-project

The panel should advise the borrower periodically on all environmental
aspects of the project, including environmental plans, procedures, budgets,

Environmental Unit's staff on training, functions, and relations with the
Ministry of Tourism and Natural Resource, the Kenya Wildlife Service, tho

governmental institutions. Panel reviews should be held twice a year during
preparation and implementation.

study area, particularly in respect to the long-term sustainability of river flow
patterns and riverine ecology, maintenance of wildlife and fish populations,
aquifer recharge and consequently the sustainability and lbng term viability
of the project. Include in the plan an estimate of operating costs and a
description of other inputs (such as training and institutional strengthening)
needed to carry it out. The In-House Environmental unit and tho
Environmental Advisory Panel should be involved in the long term monitoring.

Task 5.    Analysls of Aernatives to the Proposed Project:
29.   DescrIbe altematives that were examined in thb course of developing the
proposed project and Identify other atematives which might have achieved
the same objectives. The concept of altematives extends to other water
sources, siting, project design, selection of technologies and operating and
maintenance procedures. Alternatives should be compared in terms of
potential environmental impacts; estimated capital and operating costs;
suitability under local conditions; and likely institutional, training and
monitoring requirements. When describing the impacts of the alternative
solutions, indicate which are Irreversible or unavoidable and which can be
mitigated. To the extent possible, quantify the costs and benefits of each
alternative. Include the alternative of not carrying out the project, in order to
demonstrate environmental and socio-economic conditions without it.
30.  In this case, as appropriate, questions deserving special attention may
Include:
- The alternative recommended as first priority in the Japanese consultant
study of alternatives of September 1981, which proposes a raw water
tunnel taking floodwater from the Sabaki River and leading this to a large
storage reservoir on the Rare river for later treatment and release to the
Mombasa/Malindi water system. The reservoir would collect considerable
amounts of flood silt from the Sabaki River and would have a large
capacity. This project has the significant environmental benefit of
reducing the flood silt discharge into the sea which is presently
threatening the coral reefs and white sand beaches near Malindi, with
potential significant negative effects on tourism to the area.
- The alternative of complementing a limited additional water off-take from



-134-                          Annex 11
Page 10 of 38
Mzima Springs with the addition of water from other springs in the region,
for example the Taveta springs, which could be connected to the Mzima
pipeline at Voi wthin a few years.
- The altemative of developing a production well field based on the likely
existence of a subterranean waterfiow in the old Sabaki plaeo channel
near Baricho. (This altemative is being looked into as part of the regular
project study).
- The alternative of desalinization of sea water.
Task 6.    Evaluatlon and Recommendatlons: Development of
Management Plan: MTitgatlon measures.
31.   On the basis of the analysis and evaluation of the potential impacts and
altematives, recommend feasible and cost-effective measures to accomplish
the objectives of the project while preventing or reducing significant negatie
environmental impacts to acceptable levels, if possible. Parficular care
should be taken to ensure that the rivers, springs, dams, ecology,
appearance and wildlife will be kept as unchanged as possible both during
the construction period and after the project is implemented.
32.   Mitigating measures proposed should seek to reduce the negative
environmental Impacts of the project both during the construction period and
long-term. This would include suggestions for minimizing construction and
operation disturbance and damage to wildlfe, tourists, aesthetics, noise etc.
Operatng criteria for all involved companies, firms and contractors should
be made to this effect, Including criteria for camp locations, the use of
machiner,r and people movements in the natonal parks.
33.  The need for additional or temporary artiflcially constructed water outlets and
dams linked to the new pipeline to cater for local people, livestock and wildlife
populations during the construction period should be carefully considered.
(Attention must be given to potential environmental problems since artificial
water points often result in vegetation damage and soil erosion or become
destructive to normal animal behaviour and migratory patterns). Well
managed water points can however be a useful temporary tool for the
management of people and wildlife movements.
34.  Investigate the possibility of incorporating the Chyulu Hills in the Tsavo West
Natonal Park, thereby securing the long term protection of the water
catchment area and the river systems originating In the area, particularly the
many springs and streams feeding the Tsavo and Galana river system, and
ultimately Mombasa.
35.   Estimate the impacts and costs of all proposed msasures, and of the
institutional and training requirements to implement them. Consider
compensation to affected parties, particularly to the National Park and Wildlife
Conservation authorities, for impacts which cannot be mitigated. Prepare a
management plan including proposed work programs, budget estimates,
schedules, staffing and training requirements, and other necessary support
services to implement the mitigating measures.

B.
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR
AN ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT ASSESSEMENT OF THE PROPOSEID
SECOND MOMBASA AND COASTAL WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
(with the Mzima Springs alternative)
Introducion
1.   The purpose of this terms of reference is to guide and structure the
preparation of an environmental Impact assessment of the Second Mombasa
Water Supply Project with special reference to the alternaive of tapping
additional water from the Mzima Springs in the Tsavo West Natonal Park,
Kenya.
2.   The development project to be assessed is at the time of writing this terms
of reference not yet specMed as to the final technical alternatfve to be
developed. However, the situation leading up to the present project
considerations and pre-planning are as follows: The area of the Kenyan coast
under review for augmented water supply through the project includes
primarily greater Mombasa Osland and mainland), the north coast up to
Malindi, tie major development centres on the immediate South coast, and
the hinterand along this coastal strip Including development areas along the
present Mzlma pipeline. This area covers approximately 1000 square
kilometres and has presently (1991) an estimated population of nearl 1.7
million inhabitants.
3.   The estimated average daily demand for fresh water in this area is around
200,000 cubic meters. The demand from the Mombasa urban centre and
surroundings, which is the focus of the present project proposal, counts for
nearly 125,000 cubic meters of the above amount. However, tne present total
average public supply is less than 85,000 cubic meters of water, leaving a
deficit that means strict water rationing with consequent restrictions on
development and human use. Projections for future viater demand in the
Mombasa and surrounding area is estimated to almost 200,000 cubic meters
daily by the year 2000.
4.   The present public supply is provided from four sources;
Mzima Springs in Tsavo National Park  approx  35,000 cu.m
Marete Springs/Pemba River          to       9,000 cu.m
South Coast (Tiwi) boreholes         "       4,000 cu.m
Baricho treatment works on Sabaki River  "  35,000 cu.m
5.   The Baricho treatment works operate at 2/3 capacity while the rest work at
full capscity. The Baricho treatment works has a very high operating cost
while the Mzima Springs Pipeline has very low operating costs. Mzima
Springs provides gravity fed clean spring water while Baricho cleans and treat
extremely sift-polluted water from the river and pumps it to the consumers.



Annex I 1
-139-
Page 15 of 38
A solution to the unsatisfactory water situation and provision of adequate
fresh water supplies to satisfy the demand in Mombasa and surroundings is
considered top political and development priority at the moment.
6.    Responsibility for the executing arrangements for the environmental
assessment rests with Kenya Government as potential borrower of project
funds from the World Bank. The National Water Conservation and Pipeline
Corporation (NWCPC) represents the Kenyan Government on technical
project matters and will be the contracting partner for the consultant
undertaking the impact assessment. The World Bank's guidelines for
environmental Impact assessments will apply.
Background Informatlon
7.   The maJor components of the proposed project have not yet been agreed
upon. The requirements of the assessment may therefore be changed to
some degree. This terms of reference describes the alternative of a
two-phased coastal water supply project that includes the Mzima 11 Pipeline
In the second project phase as detailed below, assuming additional
construcUion works at the Mzima Springs, but not the constuction of any
major open wator dam or reservoir on the Tsavo or Galana rivers, and no
resettlementof people. However, the tasks outlined by the terms of reference
include reference to the need for additional studies In the cases that additlonal
works may be likely.
The first phase of the project was initially classified a C-category according
to the World Bank's Operational Directive 4.00, while the second phase is a
categoryA, needing full assessment. However, phase t, part II: Rehabiltation
and augmentation works; (see below) is a category B component to be given
limited assessment.

9.   POTENTIAL SECOND PHASE PROJECT COMPONENTS:
(needing full assessment)
A.    SOURCE WORKS.
Additional works at the Mzima Springs to accommodate increased
water abstraction ftom the springs to an indicated capacity of 1.2

be made for construction of offices, chlorine store and dosing
building, and project personnel housing.
S.   PIPES.
The laying of large diameter pipes from Mzlma to Mazeras reservoir.
This Is a distance of 219 kms. and the pipes must be able to supply
0.8 cu.m/sec. in a grevity system. Une valves, wash-outs, air valves,
access chambers, anchor blocks, thrust blocks, fittings and all other
relevant pipeline appurtenances and apparata.

expected.

New storage reservoirs and rehabilitation of old ones along the original
pipeline alignment and in the vicinity of Mombasa may be done.

The new pipeline is expected to fqllow an alignment close to the
existing pipeline. It will be necessary to rehabilitate the existng pipeline
road to gravel standard.

Water level indicators, flow meters, and a pressure measurement
system will be installed as required.

- Mazerap to Changamwe
- Mazeras to Kaya Bombo
- Mazeras to Nguu Tatu
- New pipeline to Mariakani
- Interconnections between the new and old pipeline.
10.  The need for this project and the objectives it is intended to meet appear
obvious to anybody familiar with the precarious water situation in the project
area. The urgent neqd for adequate and clean freshwater supplies to the
population and for coastal development should be met without unnecessary
delays, but the urgency must not lead to hasty and wrong decisions causing
or leading to long-term negative or damaging external impacts affecting the
sustainability of the natural resource base of the region.
11.  The implementing agency Is the National Conservation and Pipe!ind
Corporation (NWCPC). The Corporation and its institutional systems and
operatons are also in need of rehabilitation and strengthening. This is
planned as an integral part of the first phase of the project.



Annex 1 1
-142-
Page 18 of 38
12.  The project was originally put forward as one of a number of alternatives for
consideration elaborated by consultants in the early 1970's. The consultants
were at that time investigating additional sources for the augmentation of the
"Mombasa Pipeline Board Supplies", seeking alternatives to provide sufficient
additional water to meet expanding demands in Mombasa up to 1990. The
client later decided to increase the scope of the study to incorporate not only
supplies to Mombasa but to the coastal region north of Mombasa as far as
Malindi. Three alternative schemes were identified, two of which were based
upon development of the Sabaki River while the third was based on further
development of the Mzima Springs. (The existing Mzima oipeline dates back
to the early 1950's.) One of the Sabaki River alternatives was preferred and
implemented, but the project has never reached the capacity originally
foreseen for several reasons.
13.  A Japanese funded study of 20 identified alternatives for water supply to the
coastal region was finalized in 1981. This study concluded in the end that
water from the Sabaki River led into a storage reservoir at the Rare River and
from there to Mombasa after treatment was the best alternative, with Mzima
11 and a regulating dam on the lower Tsavo river as the second best
alternative.
14.  Due to various delays leading to the present critical water supply situation
on the coast which gives serious urgency to a solution, the above described
Mzlma II project together with various rehabilitation works nre now being
considered as the easiest to realize with the lowest possible operational
costs. However, recent discoveries of potentially rich waterbearing layers in
the subterranean old plaeo channel of the Sabaki River now gives rise to
optimism as to the potential for significant borehole production in the close
proximity to Baricho.
15.  Current status and timetable of the proposed project. Appraisal of the first
rehabilitation and institution building phase together with the engineering
credit facility is expected to take place in June/July 1991. The successful
negotiation of this component could release funding of the US$ 40.00 million
package by early 1992. The Mzima pipeline investment and construction
project phase could have a partly overlapping parallel development leading
to its possible conclusion by 1995 - 96.
16.  There are none associated projects plannod or in progress within the region
which may compete for the same resources, although private development
of local waterworks along the coast is a common result of the present
situation. Particularly the large tourist hotels and certain industrial complexes
are desperate to get adequate water supplies and are seeking alternative
solutions. It is not expected that this will significantly affect the proposed
project.
Objectives of the Environmental Impact Assessment.
17.  The main focus of the environmental assessment will be on the water
producing capacity of the Mzima Springs, and the effects on the downstream
vegetation, wildlife and ecology of a reduced waterfiow in the river systems



Annex I 1
-143-                       Page 19 of 38
as a resuft of the project. It will also focus on the disturbance created by
project activities on wildlife in the Tsavo National Park and the impacts on
tourism and the local population to the extent that may be significant.
18.  Most of the activities covered by the first project phase consisting of
rehabilitation works and institutional strengthening is likely to have limited or
Insignificant negative environmental impacts. This has been anticipated by
its classification as a C category project. However, since this project phase
Is likely to lead directly into, or follow parallel to the preparation and design
of the main project (second phase), the environmental impact assessment
should take place as soon as possible.
Envlronmental Assessment Requirements
19.  The regulations and guidelines which will govern the conduct of the
assessment include:
- World Bank Operational Directive 4.00, Annex A: Environmental
Assessment, and Annex B: Environmental Policy for Dam and Reservoir
Projects, and pertinent Operational Directives, Operational Policy Notes,
and Guidelines cited in Annex A2 thereto;
- National laws and/or regulations on environmental reviews and impact
assessments.
- Regional, provincial or communal environmental assessment regulations.
- Environmental assessment regulations of any othqr financing
organizations involved in the project.
Study Area
20.   The boundaries of the influence area to be studied by the assessment
Incorporates in broad terms the Chyulu Hills, Tsavo East and Tsavo West
National Parks and the area between these parks and the sea, including the
immediate off shore area. However, in a stricter sense it includes the Chyulu
Hills which are believed to be the main catchment area for the Mzima Springs,
the wider area around the Mzima Springs in the Tsavo National Park, the
areas along the Mzima River in its full length, the Tsavo River from the
confluence with the Mzima River and down to the Galana River, the Galana
(Sabaki) river from its confluence with the Tsavo River to the sea, the
immediate offshore area near the mouth of the Sabaki River that includes the
coral reefs, and the water distribution areas (to a lesser degree). The study
area along,these rivers, springs and seashore, include the areas used by
wild animats that relates to the river ecosystem and water supplies. The study
area also includes a broad belt of influence along the pipeline and related
roads, reservoirs, camps and activities.
Scope of Work
21.   The scope is primarily to analyze the environmental impacts of the project,
In particular impacts on the ecology, wildlife and vegetation that depends on
the Mzlma Springs and Mzima river, but also on the rivers and riverine
ecology further downstream and coral reefs off-shore. It will include impacts
on tourism, the local population in general terms, and the tourism economy,
conaidering also the impact an inadequate water supply may have on that



Annex I 1
-144f-_
Page 20 of 38
Industry and the population if the project is not implemented. Attention will
also be given to alternative solutions and sources, and to the stability of the
upper watershed.
22.  UImnology, hydrology and geomorphology with particular attention on the
sustainable water production and water quality of the Springs should be
investigated, and safe water off-take llmits under different climatic seasons
should be estimated with specified probability margins. Specialized field
studies or modelling activities for the Mzima Springs may have to be carried
out to secure their survival and the consultants will be asked to define the
specific tasks in more detail for contracting agency review and approval.

observations (to define a full annual cycle) is required as a minimum, and a
multi-year monitoring program established. The baseline data shall be
described in a way that they will be useful for long term monitoring and
comparison.

Task S.    Analysis of Alternatives to the Proposed Project:
28.   Describe alternatives that were examined in the course of developing the
proposed project and identify other alternatives which might have achieved
the same objectives. The concept of alternatives extends to other water
sources, siting, project design, selection of technologies and operating and
maintenance procedures. Alternatives should be compared in terms of
potential envjronmental impacts; estimated cap,lal and operating costs;
suitability under local conditions; and likely institutional, training and
monitoring requirements. When describing the impacts of the alternative
solutions, indicate which are irreversible or unavoidable and which can be
mitigated. To the extent possible, quantify the costs and benefits of each
alternative. Include the alternative of not carrying out the project, in order to
demonstrate environmental and socio-economic conditions without it.
29.  In this case, as appropriate, questions deserving special attention may
Include:



Annex I 1
-147-                         Page 23 of 38
- The atermative of just ccllecting the return flow of approximately 0.7 - 0.8
cu.m./sec. which is presently returned from the sump tunnel to the lower
Mzlma pond. This would require only a slightly larger additional pipeline
from the existing Spring outlet, and could save the whole environmentally
disturbing and potentially damaging installation of a completely new
intake structure and facilities above and adjacent to the Mzima Springs.
- The alternative of adding water from other spring sources to the Mzima
pipeline system, that could complement a limited addifional water off-take
from Mzima Sprngs within a few years. One interesting option in this
respect could be to connect a side-pipeline from the Taveta springs to
the pipeline near Voi.
- The altemative of complementing a limited additional off-take from Mzima
with the development of a production well-field based on the likely
existence of a subterranean waterfiow in the old Sabaki plaeo channel
near Baricho. (This alternative is being looked into as part of the regular
proJect study, but without adequate consideration for the second Mzima
pipeline).
- The alternative recommended as first priority in the Japanese consusant
study of alternatives of September 1981, which proposes a raw water
tunnel taking floodwater from the Sabaki River and loading this to a large
storage reservoir on the Rare river for later treatment and release to the
MombasalMalindi water system. The reservoir would collect considerable
amounts of flood silt from the Sabaki River and would have a large
capacity. This project has the significant environmental benefit of
reducing the flood silt discharge into the sea which is presently
threatening the coral reefs and white sand beaches near Malindi, with
potential significant negative effects on tourism to the area.

42.(b)     Qualtifcations:
(1)   General - All consultants must hold recognized professional
qualifications in their respective fields. The team leader should have
at least 10 years relevant work experience and the other team
members at least five years.
(i)  Team Leader (Hydrologist) - African working experience is
essential, experience in Kenya would be an advantage. Must be
experienced in various aspects of hydrology and field hydrologica
studies, the hydrological relatonship to ecology and vegeton,
environmental issues, geomorphology, water utilization and
waterworks.
(iii)  Ecologist  Africa experience mandatory. Must have broad
experience in management of natural habitats, riverine ecology,
rangeland ecology, development and human impacts on wildlife and
vegetation. Must be qualified to deal with both flora and fauna aspects
of the ecology, including river ecology and biodiversity.
(iv)  Wildlife Specialist - East African experience mandatory. Must have
broad experience in wildlife ecology and management including range
and habitat management, relocation of animals, and development
impacts on animal behaviour.
(v)  Resource Economist - African experience mandatory. Kenyan
experience desirable. Should be resource economist experienced in
assessing environmental impacts, impacts related to tourism and
natural resources, and in assigning economic values and costs where
feasible. Should be familiar with establishing costs and benefits of
proposed project activities or of measures that may be recommended
to protect the environment.
(vi)  Institutlon Specialist - experience in Africa mandatory. Work
experience in Kenya would be desirable. Must have experience in
analyzing and determining the impact of Govemment policies and
Institutional design on the relevant sector. Should have experience in
dealing with issues arising from natural resources and environmental
policies, legislation and Governmental organizational structure.
(vii) Umnologist - experience in tropical limnology. Experience with all
aspects of water analysis. Experience with estimation of water habitat
changes due to eutrophification, changed oxygen levels, changed
chemical composition, silt contents and contamination aspects.

C.
DRAFT TERMS OF REFERENCE
FOR A LIMITED ENVIRONMENTAL ASSESSMENT
OF THE SECOND MOMBASA AND COASTAL WATER SUPPLY
ENGINEERING AND REHABILITATION PROJECT
Introduction
1.    The purpose of this terms of reference is to guide and structure the preparation of a
limited environmental analysis of the Second Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Engineering
and Rehabilitation Project.
2.     The proposed Second Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Project will provide for:
(a)    institutional support for the National Water Conservation and Pipeline
Corporation (NWCPC), the implementing agency;
(b)    preinvestment studies and engineering for a follow-on main investment project
(including source works development and improvement/extension to the
distribution systems); and
(c)    rehabilitation/augmentation of existing facilities to improve the water supply in
the near term.
3.     The Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Scheme is a large regional water supply
system. The service area includes Mombasa municipality and its environs, the coastal strip
north of Mombasa up to Malindi, the south coast area, Kwale town and an about 10 km wide
strip of occupied land along the Mzima-pipeline and the Mombasa-Nairobi road and railway
line between Voi and Marere. The last extension of the system took place in 1972-79 under
the first Mombasa and Coastal Water Supply Project, with the construction of a surface water
intake oni the Sabaki river near Baricho, water treatment plant, and transmission mains to
Mombasa and other urban centers in the coast region. The project did not fulfill the
expectations. Except a short initial period, the source works did not operate at design
capacity, and due to cost overrun some of the originally planned project components
(telemetry and communication system, one of the booster pumping stations, storage tanks,
etc.) were not implemented and many components of the system are in a serious state of
disrepair. THe proposed project would provide, Inter alia, for the rehabilitation/ augmentation
of this bulk water supply system.
4.     The present water demand for Monmbasa and the coastal region is estimated at 160,000
to 170,000 m3/day, versus the available supply of about 95.000 mfday from all existing
sources.
S.     The theoretical output (design capacity) of the Sabaki/Baricho system is 55,000
r.3lVday. Tllis amount, however, cannot be obtained at present, due to problems of siltation
within the intake and due to limitations in pumping capacity. The present average production
from this source is about 41,000 m3/day.



Annex I 1
-154-                               Page 30 of 38
6.     Extensive hydrogeological investigations have been carried (under KfW financing) in
the last 5 years near Baricho for possible groundwater sources to augment the output of the
system, principally to improve the water supply in Malindi. The investigation identified the
old paleo-channel of the Sabaki river as a potential source; two large diameter wells are
presently under construction, with an expected yield of 10,000 m3/day each.
7.    The proposed project will provide for the continuation/extension of the
hydrogeological investigations. Based on the available data, it is expected that with the
construction of a new wellfield on the left-bank (flood plain) of the Sabaki river, an additional
40,000 m3/day can be made available from this source.
Environimental Imoact AsseSsMent (EAlM
8.    The project component for which this terms of reference apply, is the augmentation
rehabilitation of the existing Sabaki/Baricho bulk supply system. This component is classified
as B-category (in accordance with the World Bank's Operational Directive 4.00) needing
limited environmental impact assessment. While the responsibility for carrying out this
assessment rests with NWCPC, it will be carried out on behalf of NWCPC by the consultants
employed for the hydrogeological investigations and engineering designs of the
rehabilitation/augmentation works.
Obiectiv  o     EnviroiMental limpact Assessment
9.     The main focus of the environmental assessment will be to quantify in ecological,
economic and social terms any negative environmental impacts caused by the well-field
development, access roads, pipelines, reservoirs and associated works. The water catchment
area will be considered along with the effects on the downstream vegetation, wildlife,
ecology, aquifer recharge, subterranean river discharge, estuaries, possible salt intrusion and
impacts on nearby marine environments, and mitigating measures suggested.
Eivironmental Assessment Reequirements
11.   The regulations and guidelines which will govern the conduct of the assessment
include:
World Bank Operational Directive 4.00, Annex A: Environmental
Assessment, and Annex B: Environmental Policy for Dam and
Reservoir Projects, and pertinent Operational Directives, Operational
Policy Notes, and Guidelines.
National laws andlor regulations on environmental reviews and impact
assessments.
-      Regional, provincial or communal environmental assessment
regulations.
Enviroiinmental assessment regulations of any other financing
organizations involved in the project.



Annex 1 1
-155-                             Page 31 of 38
11.    The boundaries of the influence area to be studied by the assessment. incorporates in
broad terms an area that includes both sides of the Sabaki River from some kilometers above
Baricho, to the sea, and the immediate offshore area near the mouth of the Sabaki River that
includes the coral reefs. The study area does also include any areas used by wild animals that
relates to the river ecosystems and water supplies. The study area further includes a broad
belt of influence along pipelines and construction areas, related roads and transportation
corridors, dams, reservoirs and impoundments, treatment plants and facilities, distribution
facilities, camps and housing, and related works.
ScEoe of Work
12.    The scope is primarily to analyze the environmental impacts of the project, in
particular impacts on the riverine ecology, impacts on estuaries, beaches and coral reefs
offshore. It will include impacts on tourism, the local population in general terms, and the
tourism economy.
13.    Hydrology and geomorphology with particular attention on the sustainable water
production, water quality and water balance of the lower Sabaki area and Malindi with
surroundings should be investigated, and safe water off-take limits under different climatic
seasons should be estimated.
14.   The indirect effects, particularly the influx of population caused or facilitated by roads
and project-related facilities, and the activities of that population including settlement,
agriculture, poaching, pollution, and fuel gathering, may be more significant than the direct
effects of the project. Specialized field studies or modelling activities may have to be carried
out and the consultants are required to define the specific tasks in more detail in their
proposals.
Consultant Tasks
Task 1. Description of the oronosed Prpoject
15.    Provide a full description of the project and its general setting, as applicable:
-      The proposed project: general location and layout; size of area
involved in total and in the various components (e.g., catchment area,
drainage area, pipeline area, reservoirs areas, ponds and dams, camps
and settlements, proj"ct roads, project related facilitits, water
distribution areas, other construction activities involved, staff required
and new communities and other facilities involved; operation and
maintenance; planned public or private off-site investments as a result
of the project; life span.
k2Baseline Data - Descrintion of the Environment

the study area. The hydrologic balance of the lower Sabaki river and delta area should be
estimated. Where there are inadequate existing data, a multi-year monitoring program must

Task 4. Determinaio ofthe Potential-Impacts of the Propo -Ed P -oet
tion ofPod                           o
18.    Distinguish between significant positive and negative impacts, direct and indirect
impacts, and immediate and long term impacts. Identify impacts which are unavoidable or
irreversible. Consider cumulative impacts in cases where there are otlher planned or existing
projects with which this project may interact. Wherever possible, describe impacts
quantatively, in terms of environmental costs and benefits. Assign economic values when
feasible. Characterize the extent and quality of available data, explaining significant
information deficiencies and any uncertainties associated with predictions of impact.
19.    Among potential impacts that require particular attention in this case are the
following:
direct effects on people caused by the project - the need for
involuntary resettlement (covered by separate World Bank Operational
Directive) - unplanned people movement and settlements in the well-

field area. The long term impacts of changes in the location and
pattern of human activities caused by the project, including increased
erosion, pollution, poaching, economic activities.
the sustainability of the proposed additional off-take of water through

and impacts that may be expected.
the expected primary impacts of project construction, e.g. pipelines,
roads, reservoirs and dams, treatment and distribution facilities,
including traffic disturbance, noise, injured esthetics, increased
population movements, erosion and pollution.
human health problems, water quality, pollution, the impacts on

the effects of the flora, wildlife, ecology, biodiversity, and wildlife

the effect of additional subterranean water off-take on the ground

impact on fisheries, fish migration, estuaries and marine biota,

effects on soils and riverbanks, erosion, land loss, effects caused by

impacts on water discharge regimes to the sea with potential effects
on beaches, coral reefs, estuarine and marine life or short and near-

identify specific studies which are required to fill in needed

reference for the studies.

Task 5. Analvsis of Alternaies to the Proposed-Project
20.    Describe alternatives that were examined in the course of developing the proposed
project and identify other alternatives which might have achieved the same objectives. The
concept of alternatives extends to other water sources, silting, project design, selection of
technologies and operating and maintenanc, procedures. Alternatives should be compared in
terms of potential enviromnental impacts; estimated capital and operating costs; suitability
under local conditions; and likely institutional, training and monitoring requirements. When
describing the impacts of the alternative solutions, indicate which are irreversible or
unavoidable and which can be mitigated. To the extent possible, quantify the costs and
benefits of each alternative. Include the alternative of not carrying out the project, in order to
demonstrate environmental and socio-economic conditions without it.
Tasks 6. EvYaluation and Reco-mmendations: _Development of Managzement
Plan: -Mitigation measure
21.    On the basis of the analysis and evaluation of the potential impacts and alternatives,
recommend feasible and cost-effective measures to accomplish the objectives of the project
while preventing or reducing significant negative environmental impacts to acceptable levels,
if possible. Particular care should be taken to ensure that the down-stream (of the well-field)
rivers, ecology, appearance and wildlife will be kept as unchanged as possible both during the
construction period and after the project is implemented.
22.    Mitigating measures proposed should seek to reduce the negative environmental
impacts of the project both during the construction period and long-term. This would include
suggestions for minimizing construction and operation disturbance and damage to the local
population, tourism, esthetics, wildlife, noise etc. Operating criteria for all involved
companies, firms and contractors should be made to this effect, including criteria for camp
locations and the use of machinery and people.
23.    Estimate the impacts and costs of all proposed measures, and of the institutional and
training requirements to implement them. Consider compensation to affected parties,
particularly to the local population for impacts which cannot be mitigated. Prepare a
management plan including proposed work programs, budget estimates, schedules, staffing
and training requirements, and other necessary support services to implement the mitigating
measures.
Task 7. Identification of lnstional Need.sto Implement Environmental Assessment
RBmmendations
24.    Review the authority and capability of institutions concerned with project
implementation and environmental impacts, primarily the National Water Conservation and
Pipeline Corporation, but also the Provincial and District Administrations and recommend
steps to strengthen or expand them so that the management and monitoring plans in the
environmental assessment can be implemented. The recommendations may extend to new
laws and regulations, new agency functions, interministerial and intersectoral arrangements,
management procedures and training, staffing, operation and maintenance training, budgeting
and financial support.
25.    Recommend, if appropriate, the establishment and composition of an in-house
Environmental Unit in the National Water Conservation and Pipeline Corporation with
adequate budget and professional staffing strong in expertise relevant to the project. It should



-159-                             Anr.ex 11
Page 35 of 38
be well represented at the project site, and work in conjunction with existing government
agencies. The Unit should be established as early as possible to help ensure that pre-project
baseline data are collected and environmental problems anticipated at an early stage. The
Unit shall be established through a formal agreement with the Bank, and the Unit should
ensure that monitoring, evaluation and that mitigating measures are implemented.
26.    Review project monitoring plans and if needed, prepare a plan to evaluate the impacts
of the project and to monitor implementation of any mitigating measures that may be
recommended. This is particularly important in terms of the impact of additional water off-
take from the subterranean rivers and ground water reservoirs in the study area, particularly
in respect to the long-term sustainability of the well-field and riverine ecology, aquifer
recharge and consequently the sustainability and long term viability of the project. Include in
the plan an estimate of operating costs and a description of other inputs (such as training and
institutional strengthening) needed to carry it out. The in-house Environmental Unit should
be involved in the long term monitoring.
Task 9. Assist in Inter-Aiency Coordination and Public/NGO Participation.
27.   In addition to the evaluation of concerned agencies described in "Task 7" and as
necessary, the environmental assessment should ascertain the views of all concerned
government agencies; in collaboration with NWCPC, ensure participation in the assessment
of relevant NGO's and ascertain the views of any affected groups. At the start of the
assessment, the study team should organize inter-agency meetings in Nairobi and Mombasa to
explain the proposed assessment, determine its scope and reach agreement on the role of each
agency and procedures to follow. In addition, appropriate meetings should be held in Malindi
and other project-affected ceiiters to inform local government agencies, tourist hotel
managers, NGO's and representatives of the local communities where applicable to ensure
their participation, as needed. The traditional system of consultation should be used. Records
should be kept of meetings and other communications activities.
RB1lQm
28.    Prepare the environmental assessment report, organized according to the outline
presented in Operational Directive 4.00 Annex Al. The main text of the report should
present the findings, analysis, conclusions and recommendations of the environmental
assessment. Supporting data should be presented in the appendices. Other agency
requirements may be included as long as the following specifications are met:
-      Executive Summary including Analysis and Recommendations.
-      Policy, legal and administrative Framework.
-      Project Description and General Setting.
-      Baseline Data.
-      Significant Environmental Impacts.
-      Analysis of Alternatives.
-      Recommendations and Management Plan.
-      Environmental Management and Training.
-      Monitoring Plan.
Appendices:
-      List of EA preparers.
-      References - additional supporting data and materials used

(b) Qualifications
(i) General, All consultants must hold recognized professional
qualifications in their respective fields. The team leader
should have at least 10 years relevant work experience and the
other team members at least five years.
(ii) Team InLeader :drologist). African working experience is
essential, experience in Kenya would be an advantage. Must
be experienced in various aspects of hydrology and field
hydrological studies, the hydrological relationship to ecology
and vegetation, environmental issues, geomorphology,
borehole production, water utilization and waterworks.
(iii) Ecologist. Africa experience mandatory. Must have broad
experience in riverine ecology, coastal environments, and
development impacts on the environment. Must be qualified to deal with
all aspects of tropical flora and fauna, including river, estaurine and
coastal species.
(iv)  Resource Economist. African experience mandatory. Kenyan
experience desirable. Should be resource economist
experienced in assessing environiental impacts, impacts
related to water, natural resources and tourism, and in
assigning economic values and costs where feasible. Should be
familihr with establishing costs and benefits of proposed
project activities and of measures that may be recommended to
protect the environmenit.
(v)   Institution Specialist. Expcrience in Africa mandatory. Work
experience in Kenya would be desirable. Must have
experience in analyzing and determining the impact of
Government policies and institutional design on the relevant
sector. Should have experience in dealing with issues arising

NATIONAL WATER CONSERVATION AND PMELINE. CORPORATION
ASSUMPTIONS FOR FINANCIAL PRO               QINS
.COME STATEMENTS
1.     Available Water Supply. Assumes an increase of 20,000 m3Mday in FY92/93 with
completic;i of a German-funded augmentation scheme. An additional 40,000 m'lday is estimated
to become available mid-way through FY93194 with completion of the proposed project.
2.     Water Demand. Assumes 3.5% growth annually, in line with the national growth rate.
3.     Nu mr qf.CQnnW!tiQns. Assumes 3.5% growth annually, in line with the natiors.l
growth rate.
4.     LVater Salts. Average tariff multiplied by the annual water sales volume.
5.     Miscellanegus Receipts. Assumes a modest increase of 2% growth per year.
6.     S1aE      Includes a decrease of about 1/3 during FY91/92 corresponding to the decrease
in staff which were returned to the Ministry of Water Development, and assumes a 20% increase
annually thereafter.
7.     AdmiCisfratign. Assumes a 10% increase per year.
8.         olas- Oerating-Expnses. Assumes a 20% increase per year from FY91192 through
FY93194 and a 10% increase per year thereafter.
9.    jjfr  tT . Assumes a 20% increase per year.
10.    X.I. Assumes a 10% increase per year.
11.      5wigaI5. Assumes current average cost of KSh 0.385Imn Increases by about 20% per
year and Is multiplied by the volume of water sold.
12.    Mab%n=JS  . Assumes an increase of 20% per year to bring the rundown facilities into
better operating condition.
13.       D     S4y           Assumed at 10% of accounts receivables, in line with financial
policy.
14.    12Cgrjim. Assumed at 3% of gross fixed assets aHW assuming that total assets
transferred to NWCPC will be valued by end-FY92193. A notional value of KSSh 10,800,000,00
(US$400,000,000) for the fixed assets transferred has been included in the analysis in the
meantime.
15.     tci  . Assumes NWCPC will pay 16.5% on borfowings to be repaid over 20 years
with a S-year grace period on repayment of principal, corresponding to the implementation period
of the project.

BAL&E1
1.     Gross FixedA=. Includes notional value of assets transferred to NWCPC as well as
the proposed project and other ongoing projects in the year of commissioning. The cost of
technical assistance and training is also included annually.
2.    WYrkwin-PE  gress. Includes construction costs of all projects and associated engineering
services until commissioning.
3.     Accounts Receivable. Assumes number of days of receivables will decrease gradually
from about 225 days of billings during FY90/91 to:
FY91192       200 days
FY92/93       180 days
FY93/94       150 days
FY94195       120 days
FY95196        90 days
4.     IIpentoris. Assumed at 25% of annual costs of fuel, chemicals and maintenance
expenditures.
5.     Accounts Payal. Assumed at 3 months of expenditures during FY91/92 and at 1 month
of expenditures thereafter.
6.     Customers' Deposits. Estimated at about KSh 14,701,000 as of end-FY90191 and
estimated to grow at 3.5% per year thereafter.
E              tCE_S AND ALCATIONS OF EUNDSSATMENTS
1.     Government Grant - Proiect. Assumes Government will provide 11% of the total cost of
the project as an equity contribution to NWCPC.
2.     Governnent- Ga    t -Oe    ok. Assumes Government will provide 100% of the cost
of other ongoing projects, previously arranged, as an equity contribiition to NWCPC.
3.     Gioernment G   r      a   k    r. Assumes Government will transfer all assets to
NWCPC as an equity contribution.
4.     BoMftings.       C      Assumes 89% of the cost of the project will be financed by the
IDA Credit.
5.     =orrowingS - Donors. -bturePrect. Assumes 90% of the cost of the future project
will be financod by donors with the bal ace of 10% to be provided by NWCPC.

SECOND MOMBASA AND COASTAL WATER SUPPLY
ENGINEERING AMD REHABILITATION PROBECT
Projegt Monitoring Guidelines
General
1.    The following guidelines were prepared to facilitate planning and to evaluate achievement of
technical, financial, management and training goals. These will be included in quarterly reports to be
submitted to the Association. Performance targets for indicators (where applicable) are shown on the
attachment to this annex.
Monitoring Guidelines
2.     NWCPC would develop and maintain records on the following:
A.       at  stemOperation Dgta
1.    Water production: daily production at source works, water level recording in
the reservoirs, metered production at the treatment plant(s);
2.     Water consumption: metered monthly consumption and revenues billed for all
categories of water demand and consumption blocks including, but not
restricted to, the following uses: (i) residential; (ii) commercial, industrial,
and (iii) governmental/institutional.
3.     Unaccounted for water by month.
4.     Numbers of connections listed by consumer category and consumption blocks,
including residential connections of the following types: (i) private
connections; (ii) public standposts; (iii) water selling kiosks; and (iv) others.
5.    Interruptions in service: the extent to which water service was interrupted on a
quarterly basis with specific details of: (a) frequency, (b) duration, and (c)
cause of interruption.
1.    Quarterly forecasts of expenditures are to be prepared for each contract within
thlree mondhs of signiature of contract. Quarterly reports are to indicate

ANNEX I5
Page 2 of 4
percentage completion of original forecasts and revised forecast of expenditure
for remainder of contract.
2.     Descriptive report on progress with the implementation of the individual
project components (including progress on consultancies and physical works
on the rehabilitation/augmentation component.
C.     Financiat Indicators (required annually)
1.    Increase in Water Tariff (Coast Region - other regions);
2.     Growth in Water Revenues (Coast Region - other regions);
3.     Value of Gross Fixed Assets (with special regard to the progress of valuation
of assets and liabilities);
4.     Retum on Net Fixed Assets;
5.     Interest coverage;
6.     Current ratio;
7.     Operating ratio;
8.     Accounts receivable;
9.     Collection performance by category of customier; and
10.    Six-years projection of NWCPC's financial perfornmance.
D.     Staffing and TUrinin2
1.     Sltffng (end of financial year)
(a) Number of permanent employees;
(i)   Professional,
(ii)   Subprofessional.
2.     Train!in
(i)    Number of staff trained during financial year by category duration,
and type of training (viz., on-the-job, off-the-job),
(ii)   Detailed training program for next fiscal year.
